spectra were obtained with IMACS and FIRE and extracted to mimic a circular aperture with <em>R</em>\u00a0=\u00a02<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />2. Wavelengths are in vacuum in the observed frame. Pixels excluded in our fits have a flag of 0. The instrumental resolution is provided in terms of the Gaussian <em>\u03c3</em>.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Only a portion of this table is shown here to demonstrate its form and content. A <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"webref\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"/0004-637X/845/2/157/suppdata/apjaa816dt2_mrt.txt\">machine-readable</a> version of the full table is available.</p>\n\n\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-table-data\" href=\"/0004-637X/845/2/157/suppdata/apjaa816dt2_mrt.txt\" target=\"_blank\">Data</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/845/2/157/suppdata/apjaa816dt2_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa816ds8\" name=\"apjaa816ds8\">8.\u00a0Summary</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We have compared lensing, dynamical, and SPS-based methods of estimating the IMF in the three low-redshift strong lenses located in the SNELLS survey. All were shown by Smith et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib69\">2015b</a>) to have a relatively lightweight IMF based on their lensing masses, in contrast to the heavier IMFs claimed to be typical of similarly high-<em>\u03c3</em> ETGs based on earlier studies. We have investigated whether this discrepancy arises from scatter in the IMF of high-<em>\u03c3</em> ETGs or from a fundamental inconsistency among the methods. To do so, we analyzed new spectroscopic observations using new SPS models. Our principal findings are as follows.</p><ul class=\"clear-list wd-inline-list\"><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">1.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">Comparing lensing/dynamical estimates of the IMF mass factor <em>\u03b1</em> to SPS-based estimates derived from full spectral fitting, we find that all methods are consistent, regardless of the modeling assumptions, for one lens (SNL-2). For the other two lenses, the comparison depends on the dark matter content and the IMF parameterization used in the modeling.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">2.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">For IMFs with one or two power-law segments extending over 0.08\u20131 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>, the spectroscopic <em>\u03b1</em> exceeds the lensing mass for SNL-0 and SNL-1. The significance depends on the dark matter fraction, but is 1.9\u20132.5<em>\u03c3</em> even if no dark matter is assumed.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">3.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">When adopting IMF parameterizations with more flexibility at low stellar masses, the tension among methods within the SNELLS sample decreases to 1.5<em>\u03c3</em> (SNL-0) and 1.9<em>\u03c3</em> (SNL-1) for a fiducial estimate of the dark matter content (based on the EAGLE simulations) and to &lt;1<em>\u03c3</em> in the limit with no dark matter. Thus, in the context of the CvD models, consistency at the &lt;2<em>\u03c3</em> level among all methods of IMF estimation requires that the SNELLS galaxies, on average, have an elevated low-mass cutoff in the IMF or a turnover at low stellar masses <em>m</em>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a00.3 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">4.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">The IMFs of the SNELLS lenses are systematically lighter than the mean matched-<em>\u03c3</em> ETG as inferred by the ATLAS<sup>3D</sup>\u00a0and SLACS surveys. This difference is evident in the lensing masses regardless of the dark matter fraction. If we instead adopt the total dynamical mass <em>and</em> assume that no dark matter is present, the <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em> of the SNELLS lenses would be typical, but we argue that with current data the dynamical constraints are less precise and robust. This suggests that there may be substantial scatter in the IMF among high-<em>\u03c3</em> galaxies.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">5.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">The mean spectrum of the SNELLS lenses is very similar to an SDSS composite spectrum of matched-<em>\u03c3</em> ETGs, but subtle differences are detected at moderate statistical significance in the SPS analysis. If taken at face value, the spectral modeling suggests a deficit of low-mass stars in the SNELLS sample (either via an elevated low-mass cutoff, or a turnover at low masses in our nonparameteric models) but not for the SDSS composite. In addition, a truncated IMF is <em>not</em> needed to reconcile spectroscopic and lensing/dynamical IMF estimates for the typical matched-<em>\u03c3</em> ETG, unlike the SNELLS lenses. Therefore, variation in the form of the IMF at low stellar masses provides a plausible origin of the different lensing/dynamical IMF estimates of the two samples.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">6.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">The SNELLS galaxies do not differ systematically in size, luminosity, mass density, metallicity, or abundance pattern from the mean matched-<em>\u03c3</em> ETG, so any scatter in the IMF that may be present does not appear to correlate strongly with global galaxy properties.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">7.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">The absolute <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em> inferred from SPS modeling depends on the constraints used, particularly the inclusion of the Na <span class=\"small-caps\">i</span> 8190\u00a0\u00c5\u00a0feature. Omitting this constraint could reconcile IMF estimates within the SNELLS sample, but at the price of breaking the consistency found for the typical ETG.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">8.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">We provide fully reduced spectra of the SNELLS lenses in order to facilitate future comparisons among SPS models.</div></li></ul><p>\n</p></div> <div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We thank the referee for a thorough and thoughtful report that improved this work. We thank Lindsay Oldham for discussing unpublished observations. This paper includes data gathered with the 6.5 m Magellan Telescopes located at Las Campanas Observatory, Chile. The FORS2 and X-shooter data sets used in this paper are available through the ESO data archives (program 095.B-0736). R.J.S.\u00a0acknowledges support from the STFC through grant ST/L00075X/1. C.C.\u00a0acknowledges support from NASA grant NNX15AK14G, NSF grant AST-1313280, and the Packard Foundation. A.V.\u00a0acknowledges the support of the NSF Graduate Research Fellowship.</p></div><h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa816dapp1\" name=\"apjaa816dapp1\">Appendix A: Comparison of 6dF and Literature Velocity Dispersions</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>As described in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa816ds4-4\">4.4</a>, the velocity dispersions measured in this paper are systematically lower than those in the 6dF catalog (Campbell et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib9\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib9\">2014</a>) by ~40\u00a0km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup>. Here we investigate this discrepancy further. Our measurements are based on new observations with <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />10\u201320<b>&#x00d7;</b> higher signal-to-noise ratio than the 6dF spectra, and we obtain consistent <em>\u03c3</em> estimates from both the IMACS and X-shooter data using multiple fitting codes and templates. We are therefore confident in the new measurements and attribute the differences to a bias affecting the 6dF dispersions. To demonstrate this, we follow the selection criteria used by <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib69\">S15</a> to define the SNELLS parent sample. Specifically, we choose systems with <em>\u03c3</em><sub>6dF</sub>\u00a0&gt;\u00a0300\u00a0km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup> and a signal-to-noise ratio of at least 15\u00a0\u00c5<sup>\u22121</sup> in their 6dF spectrum. (We make no cut on environment for this test.) These criteria identify only 49 systems out of the 11315 galaxies with measured velocity dispersions in the Campbell et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib9\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib9\">2014</a>) catalog, i.e., the most extreme 0.4% of the <em>\u03c3</em><sub>6dF</sub> distribution. Of these, we have identified seven galaxies with independent <em>\u03c3</em> measurements from the SDSS DR13 (SDSS Collaboration et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib63\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib63\">2016</a>), Smith et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib70\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib70\">2000</a>), or Jorgensen et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib32\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib32\">1995</a>). These are compared to the 6dF dispersions in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa816df10\">10</a>, after standardizing to the 6<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />7 diameter 6dF aperture using the correction described in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa816ds4-4\">4.4</a>. In all cases, <em>\u03c3</em><sub>6dF</sub> exceeds the literature value. Furthermore, the mean excess <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816dieqn36.gif\" alt=\"$\\langle {\\sigma }_{6\\mathrm{dF}}-{\\sigma }_{\\mathrm{lit}}\\rangle =54\\pm 14\\,\\mathrm{km}\\,{{\\rm{s}}}^{-1}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\langle {\\sigma }_{6\\mathrm{dF}}-{\\sigma }_{\\mathrm{lit}}\\rangle =54\\pm 14\\,\\mathrm{km}\\,{{\\rm{s}}}^{-1}</script></span></span> is consistent with the mean difference between the 6dF measurements of the SNELLS lenses and those presented in this paper, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816dieqn37.gif\" alt=\"$\\langle {\\sigma }_{6\\mathrm{dF}}-{\\sigma }_{\\mathrm{SNELLS}}\\rangle =44\\,\\mathrm{km}\\,{{\\rm{s}}}^{-1}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\langle {\\sigma }_{6\\mathrm{dF}}-{\\sigma }_{\\mathrm{SNELLS}}\\rangle =44\\,\\mathrm{km}\\,{{\\rm{s}}}^{-1}</script></span></span>. We conclude that the differences between our <em>\u03c3</em> measurements and the 6dF values used by <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib69\">S15</a> are typical, and that they reflect a bias that arises when selecting galaxies in the tail of the <em>\u03c3</em> distribution of the 6dF catalog. Although it is beyond the scope of this paper to examine this bias in detail, we note that it does not necessarily imply any error in the 6dF catalogs, since part or all of the observed effect must be the Eddington bias (see Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa816ds4-4\">4.4</a>), which is purely statistical.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa816df10\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa816df10\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816df10_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 10.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 10.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816df10_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 10.</strong>\u00a0Velocity dispersions of galaxies selected from the 6dF catalog with <em>\u03c3</em><sub>6dF</sub>\u00a0&gt;\u00a0300 km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup> are compared to the measurements from this paper (diamonds) and from the literature sources (circles) described in the text. All data are corrected to a 6<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />7 diameter aperture.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaa816df10\" href=\"/0004-637X/845/2/157/downloadFigure/figure/apjaa816df10\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaa816df10\" href=\"/0004-637X/845/2/157/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaa816df10\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div><h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa816dapp2\" name=\"apjaa816dapp2\">Appendix B: Influence of Na <span class=\"small-caps\">i</span> on the Inferred IMF</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>\n<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib21\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib21\">CvD12</a> showed that the normalization of the <em>\u03b1</em> values derived from their models is very sensitive to the inclusion of the Na <span class=\"small-caps\">i</span> 8190\u00a0\u00c5\u00a0feature in the fit. Smith et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib67\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib67\">2015a</a>) showed that these models, when constrained by the remainder of the optical-to-<em>J</em>-band spectrum, fail to match the strength of the Na <span class=\"small-caps\">i</span> 1.14 <em>\u03bc</em>m line. Similarly, Meneses-Goytia et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib48\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib48\">2015</a>) found that their models had difficulty reproducing the observed strength of the Na <span class=\"small-caps\">i</span> 2.21 <em>\u03bc</em>m feature in massive ETGs. Recently, La Barbera et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib39\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib39\">2017</a>) have claimed to fit the strength of all optical-NIR Na <span class=\"small-caps\">i</span> features consistently for the first time. Nonetheless, uncertainty about the accuracy of some models in reproducing the Na <span class=\"small-caps\">i</span> features motivates an examination of the influence of sodium in our IMF measurements.</p><p>Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa816df11\">11</a> reproduces Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa816df4\">4</a> with additional open symbols that show the results obtained when the Na <span class=\"small-caps\">i</span> 8190\u00a0\u00c5\u00a0feature is excluded from the fit. Excluding Na <span class=\"small-caps\">i</span> 8190\u00a0\u00c5\u00a0reduces the inferred <em>\u03b1</em> by <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />30% on average, consistent with findings by <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib21\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib21\">CvD12</a>. Such a reduction is sufficient to remove all tension between lensing/dynamical and spectroscopic IMF constraints in the SNELLS sample. However, as seen in this figure and discussed in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa816ds7\">7</a>, masking Na <span class=\"small-caps\">i</span> introduces substantial tension among these IMF constraints when the typical matched-<em>\u03c3</em> ETG is considered. Regardless of the IMF parameterization, the SPS-derived <em>\u03b1</em> values fall significantly below the lensing/dynamics constraints when Na <span class=\"small-caps\">i</span> 8190\u00a0\u00c5\u00a0is masked. The exclusion of this feature due to potential systematic errors mainly rescales all SPS-derived <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em> downward uniformly. Therefore, it cannot reproduce the separation seen between the SNELLS galaxies and the SDSS stack in their lensing/dynamics-based <em>\u03b1</em>, which is the main subject of this paper. As an aside, we note that although masking Na <span class=\"small-caps\">i</span> 8190\u00a0\u00c5\u00a0pushes the SDSS stack to Kroupa-like <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> values, this does not necessarily eliminate the spectroscopic evidence for systematic IMF variations: this question depends on how the inference changes for lower-<em>\u03c3</em> galaxies, which is beyond the scope of this paper.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa816df11\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa816df11\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816df11_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 11.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 11.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816df11_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 11.</strong>\u00a0Reproduction of Figure <a href=\"#apjaa816df4\">4</a>, with open circles added to represent the <em>\u03b1</em> inferred when the Na <span class=\"small-caps\">i</span> feature at 8190\u00a0\u00c5\u00a0is excluded from the fit.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div></div><!-- End article full text -->\n<!-- Start Footnotes -->\n\t<h2 id=\"footnotes\">Footnotes</h2>\n\t\t<div data-mobile-collapse=\"\">\n\t\t\t<ul class=\"clear-list wd-content-footnotes\">\n\t\t\t\t<li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaa816dfn1\" id=\"apjaa816dfn1\">6\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>At these distances, the galaxy surface brightness is only <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />0.2% of its average value with the extraction aperture described below, so contamination is negligible.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaa816dfn2\" id=\"apjaa816dfn2\">7\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>The circularized radius is the radius of a circle with equal area to the isophotal ellipse passing through a point.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaa816dfn3\" id=\"apjaa816dfn3\">8\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>The SNELLS galaxies' <em>R</em><sub><em>e</em></sub>, <em>L</em>, and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816dieqn26.gif\" alt=\"${I}_{e}=L/(2\\pi {R}_{e}^{2})$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{I}_{e}=L/(2\\pi {R}_{e}^{2})</script></span></span> span the range of the parent sample defined by <em>\u03c3</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a0300 km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup>. Turning to three-parameter correlations and examining their positions through the 6dF fundamental plane (Magoulas et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib42\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib42\">2012</a>), the SNELLS galaxies are marginally displaced to high <em>I</em><sub><em>e</em></sub> at fixed <em>\u03c3</em> and <em>R</em><sub><em>e</em></sub>. Such an offset is expected for galaxies with low <em>M</em>/<em>L</em>, since \u0394<em>I</em><sub><em>e</em></sub>\u00a0\u221d\u00a0\u0394 (<em>M</em>/<em>L</em>)<sup>\u22121</sup>\u00a0\u221d\u00a0\u0394(<em>L</em>/<em>\u03c3</em><sup>2</sup><em>R</em><sub><em>e</em></sub>) at fixed <em>\u03c3</em> and <em>R</em><sub><em>e</em></sub>.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaa816dfn4\" id=\"apjaa816dfn4\">9\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>In the case of multiple stellar populations with distinct <em>M</em><sub><em>i</em></sub> and <em>L</em><sub><em>i</em></sub>, which can also represent the case of a radial <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> gradient, the spectroscopic method will approximately measure a luminosity-weighted <em>M</em>/<em>L</em>, i.e., \u03a3[(<em>M</em>/<em>L</em>)<sub><em>i</em></sub><em>L</em><sub><em>i</em></sub>]/\u03a3<em>L</em><sub><em>i</em></sub>. Lensing measures \u03a3<em>M</em><sub><em>i</em></sub>/\u03a3<em>L</em><sub><em>i</em></sub>, which is precisely equivalent.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaa816dfn5\" id=\"apjaa816dfn5\">10\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>The spectra cover the Wing\u2013Ford band for SNL-0 and SNL-1, but not the SDSS stack. We verified that this difference is not the cause of the different posteriors in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa816df8\">8</a> by repeating the analysis with the Wing\u2013Ford band masked.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaa816dfn6\" id=\"apjaa816dfn6\">11\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>We emphasize that contrary to some other usage in the literature, \"single\" and \"double\" refer to the number of power-law segments below 1 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>; at &gt;1 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> we always use the Salpeter slope.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaa816dfn7\" id=\"apjaa816dfn7\">12\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>This comparison assumes that ATLAS<sup>3D</sup>/SLACS surveys and our SDSS stack comprise representative samples of ETGs at a given <em>\u03c3</em>, which is likely the case, and that they sample comparable apertures; although the latter is not exactly true, the differences are not very large. This is particularly true when comparing SLACS, which samples <em>R</em><sub>Ein</sub>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a0<em>R</em><sub><em>e</em></sub>/2, to our SDSS stack, which samples <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" /><em>R</em><sub><em>e</em></sub>/3 on average.</p></p></div></li></ul>\n\t\t</div>\n\t<!-- End Footnotes -->\t\n<!-- Start References List -->\n\n<div class=\"reveal-container reveal-closed <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The American Astronomical Society (AAS), established in 1899 and based in Washington, DC, is the major organization of professional astronomers in North America. Its membership of about 7,000 individuals also includes physicists, mathematicians, geologists, engineers, and others whose research and educational interests lie within the broad spectrum of subjects comprising contemporary astronomy. The mission of the AAS is to enhance and share humanity's scientific understanding of the universe.</p> <p><a href=\"https://aas.org/\">https://aas.org/</a></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<!-- Partner logo tile starts -->\n<a href=\"\" class=\"overlay-launch\">\n    <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n</a>\n<span class=\"overlay-set\">\n    <div class=\"tint-screen\"></div>\n    <div class=\"overlay-panel\">\n        <a title=\"Close\"  class=\"close-icon close-overlay\" tabindex=\"0\" role=\"button\" aria-describedby=\"close-icon-description\"><span class=\"icon-close\"></span></a>\n        <p id=\"close-icon-description\" class=\"offscreen-hidden\">Click here to close this overlay, or press the \"Escape\" key on your keyboard.</p>\n        <div class=\"overlay-img\">\n            <a href=\"\">\n                <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n            </a>\n        </div>\n        <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The Institute of Physics (IOP) is a leading scientific society promoting physics and bringing physicists together for the benefit of all. It has a worldwide membership of around 50 000 comprising physicists from all sectors, as well as those with an interest in physics. It works to advance physics research, application and education; and engages with policy makers and the public to develop awareness and understanding of physics. Its publishing company, IOP Publishing, is a world leader in professional scientific communications.</p> <p><a href=\"https://www.iop.org\">https://www.iop.org</a></p></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<p></p>\n                </div>\n        </div>\n    </div>\n<!-- Branded journal header ends -->\n</div> <!-- end secondary-header -->\n<!-- Secondary header ends --><div class=\"da1-da2\" itemscope=\"\" itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle\" id=\"page-content\">\n            <div class=\"da1 ta1 article-head\">\n                <!-- Start Eyebrow block, containing Surtitle and Labels -->\n<div class=\"eyebrow\">\n\t<!-- Start Collection Labels -->\n    <!-- End Collection Labels --> </div>\n<!-- End Eyebrow block -->\n\n<h1 itemprop=\"headline\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-title\">The Initial Mass Function in the Nearest Strong Lenses from SNELLS: Assessing the Consistency of Lensing, Dynamical, and Spectroscopic Constraints</h1>\n\n<p class=\"mb-0\">\n\t<span data-authors=\"\">\n\t\t<span itemtype=\"http://schema.org/Person\" itemprop=\"author\" class=\"nowrap\"><span itemprop=\"name\">Andrew B. <span class=\"icon-newtab\">\n            </a></p>\n        </div>\n\n    <!-- End Email Alert -->\n<!-- End Email Alert --><!-- Start Citation Alert Link -->\n                        <p>\n<div class=\"jnl-notifications-wrapper\">\n    <a class=\"wd-jnl-email-alert\" href=\"https://myiopscience.iop.org/signin?origin=a0&return=https%3A%2F%2Fiopscience.iop.org%2Fmyiopscience%2Falerts%2Fsubscribe%3FarticleID%3D0004-637X%2F845%2F2%2F157\"><span\n            class=\"icon-alarm icon-alarm--jhp\"></span>Create citation alert</a>\n</div><!-- End Citation Alert Link -->\n                    </div>\n\t\t</div>\n    </div>\n    <!-- Article information ends -->\n<!-- Start Info top-->\n    <!-- end Info top --><div class=\"article-content\">\n\t<span id=\"articleId\" class=\"hide\">0004-637X/845/2/157</span>\n<!-- Start Abstract -->\n    <h2 id=\"artAbst\" class=\"collapse-blocked\">Abstract</h2>\n        <!-- Start article video abstract -->\n\t<!-- End article video abstract -->\n\t<div class=\"article-text wd-jnl-art-abstract cf\" itemprop=\"description\">\n             <P>We present new observations of the three nearest early-type galaxy (ETG) strong lenses discovered in the SINFONI Nearby Elliptical Lens Locator Survey (SNELLS). Based on their lensing masses, these ETGs were inferred to have a stellar initial mass function (IMF) consistent with that of the Milky Way, not the bottom-heavy IMF that has been reported as typical for high-<I>\u03c3</I> ETGs based on lensing, dynamical, and stellar population synthesis techniques. We use these unique systems to test the consistency of IMF estimates derived from different methods. We first estimate the stellar <I>M</I> <SUB>*</SUB>/<I>L</I> using lensing and stellar dynamics. We then fit high-quality optical spectra of the lenses using an updated version of the stellar population synthesis models developed by Conroy &amp; van Dokkum. When examined individually, we find good agreement among these methods for one galaxy. The other two galaxies show 2\u20133<I>\u03c3</I> tension with lensing estimates, depending on the dark matter contribution, when considering IMFs that extend to 0.08 <I>M</I> <SUB>\u2299</SUB>. Allowing a variable low-mass cutoff or a nonparametric form of the IMF reduces the tension among the IMF estimates to &lt;2<I>\u03c3</I>. There is moderate evidence for a reduced number of low-mass stars in the SNELLS spectra, but no such evidence in a composite spectrum of matched-<I>\u03c3</I> ETGs drawn from the SDSS. Such variation in the form of the IMF at low stellar masses (<I>m</I> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 0.3 <I>M</I> <SUB>\u2299</SUB>), if present, could reconcile lensing/dynamical and spectroscopic IMF estimates for the SNELLS lenses and account for their lighter <I>M</I> <SUB>*</SUB>/<I>L</I> relative to the mean matched-<I>\u03c3</I> ETG. We provide the spectra used in this study to facilitate future comparisons.</P> </div>\n    <!-- End abstract -->\n<!-- Start Export citation and abstract tools -->\n\t<p>\n\t\t<small>Export citation and abstract</small>\n\t\t<span class=\"btn-multi-block\">\n\t\t\t<a href=\"/export?articleId=0004-637X/845/2/157&doi=10.3847/1538-4357/aa816d&exportFormat=iopexport_bib&exportType=abs&navsubmit=Export+abstract\" class=\"btn btn-primary wd-btn-cit-abs-bib\" title=\"Export BibTex\">BibTeX</a>\n\t\t\t<a href=\"/export?articleId=0004-637X/845/2/157&doi=10.3847/1538-4357/aa816d&exportFormat=iopexport_ris&exportType=abs&navsubmit=Export+abstract\" class=\"btn btn-primary wd-btn-cit-abs-ris\" title=\"Export RIS\">RIS</a>\n\t\t</span>\n\t</p>\n<!-- End Export citation and abstract tools -->\n<!-- Start AAS Related Links -->\n<div class=\"reveal-container reveal-closed reveal-plus-icon bdt-1 mt-2 mb-2 wd-jnl-art-article-aas-related-links\">\n  <div class=\"replica-h3 mt-0\">\n    <a href=\"#\" class=\"reveal-trigger\" data-reveal-label-alt=\"Hide related links\">Related links</a>\n  </div>\n  <div class=\"reveal-content\">\n    <div class=\"article-meta\"> \n      <ul>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2017ApJ...845..157N\" target=\"_blank\">NASA ADS Record <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://nedwww.ipac.caltech.edu/cgi-bin/nph-objsearch?refcode=2017ApJ...845..157N&search_type=Search \" target=\"_blank\">NED Objects <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://simbad.u-strasbg.fr/simbad/sim-ref?querymethod=bib&simbo=on&bibcode=2017ApJ...845..157N\" target=\"_blank\">Simbad Objects <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"/journal/0004-637X/page/About%20Related%20Links\" target=\"_blank\">About Related Links</a></li>\n      \t</ul>\n    </div>\n  </div>\n</div>\n<!-- Start info bottom -->\n    <!-- End info bottom -->\n<!-- Start article full text -->\n\t<div xmlns:book=\"http://api.iop.org/Book/1.0/\" itemprop=\"articleBody\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-full-text article-text\">\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa816ds1\" name=\"apjaa816ds1\">1.\u00a0Introduction</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>The stellar initial mass function (IMF) is both fundamental as a basic outcome of the star formation process and a critical ingredient for interpreting numerous extragalactic observations. Although the IMF is consistent with being independent of the environment within the Milky Way (e.g., Bastian et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib6\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib6\">2010</a>), there is little theoretical reason for this to hold across all star-forming regions where the physical conditions can vary widely (e.g., Hennebelle &amp; Chabrier <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib29\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib29\">2008</a>; Krumholz et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib35\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib35\">2011</a>; Hopkins <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib30\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib30\">2012</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib31\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib31\">2013</a>; Chabrier et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib15\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib15\">2014</a>). Since only the brightest stars beyond the local volume can be individually detected, constraints on the IMF in external systems are challenging and must rely on integrated light observations.</p><p>Two such approaches have been applied to early-type galaxies (ETGs). The first is to model the total mass distribution using gravitational lensing, stellar kinematics, or both in combination (Auger et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib4\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib4\">2010b</a>; Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib77\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib77\">2010</a>; Spiniello et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib72\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib72\">2011</a>; Thomas et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib75\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib75\">2011</a>; Barnab\u00e8 et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib5\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib5\">2013</a>; Cappellari et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib13\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib13\">2013a</a>; Conroy et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib17\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib17\">2013</a>; Newman et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib51\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib51\">2013a</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib52\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib52\">2013b</a>). Since the radial distributions and shapes of the dark matter and stars are thought to be distinct, it is possible to separate the components with the aid of a few assumptions. The principal assumptions are that the stellar mass density follows the luminosity density and that the dark matter profile follows a parameterized form, such as a power law or an NFW halo (Navarro-Frenk-White, Navarro et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib49\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib49\">1996</a>). This leads to an integral constraint on the IMF, i.e., <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em>. A promising alternative route to <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em> is to measure the \"graininess\" of the gravitational potential via microlensing-induced flux anomalies, which Schechter et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib60\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib60\">2014</a>) have demonstrated using ten multiply imaged quasars.</p><p>The second method is based on a detailed analysis of absorption line spectra. Recent stellar population synthesis (SPS) models have opened up studies of the chemical abundance patterns and the IMF in unresolved old populations at a remarkable level of detail (Conroy &amp; van Dokkum <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib20\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib20\">2012a</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib21\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib21\">2012b</a>; van Dokkum &amp; Conroy <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib82\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib82\">2012</a>; La Barbera et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib36\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib36\">2013</a>; Spiniello et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib73\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib73\">2014</a>). While varying the age and metallicity imparts large and well-known changes to integrated galaxy spectra, variations in the IMF also affect surface gravity-sensitive absorption lines at levels that are subtle but detectable (<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />1%) with high-quality data.</p><p>When these two methods are applied to ETGs, both are consistent with a trend of \"heavier\" IMFs in higher-<em>\u03c3</em> (Cappellari et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib13\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib13\">2013a</a>; La Barbera et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib37\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib37\">2015</a>), more metal-rich (Mart\u00edn-Navarro et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib45\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib45\">2015c</a>), or more <em>\u03b1</em>-enhanced (Conroy &amp; van Dokkum <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib21\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib21\">2012b</a>) galaxies. (Since these quantities are mutually correlated, the primary driver of the correlation is more difficult to establish.) The \"heaviness\" of the IMF can be expressed via the mass factor <em>\u03b1</em>\u00a0=\u00a0(<em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em>)/(<em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em>)<sub>MW</sub>, where (<em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em>)<sub>MW</sub> is inferred from SPS models assuming a fiducial IMF and thereby normalizes differences in age or metallicity. Lensing and dynamical methods infer higher <em>\u03b1</em> in high-<em>\u03c3</em> ETGs, but cannot distinguish between an increased number of low-mass stars versus stellar remnants, while absorption line studies of such galaxies directly point to a larger number of low-mass (&lt;1 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>) stars, i.e., a \"bottom-heavy\" IMF.</p><p>This apparent convergence of entirely independent techniques has lent credence to claims of a non-universal IMF. Nonetheless, although the global IMF trends may appear consistent, Smith (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib66\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib66\">2014</a>) pointed out that some puzzles remain: the IMF constraints obtained for individual galaxies using different techniques do not always correlate well, and the principal galaxy property that underlies the IMF trends is not consistently inferred. Such comparisons depend on the data set and methods used; Lyubenova et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib41\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib41\">2016</a>) found a stronger correlation between total dynamical mass and SPS-based mass.</p><p>Such discrepancies could indicate fundamental problems in the methods used to infer the IMF. Alternatively, they could arise from difficulties in directly comparing measurements (e.g., mismatched apertures in galaxies that may have radial IMF gradients, or different IMF parameterizations used in SPS modeling), or they could point to the presence of a second parameter driving IMF variations. Distinguishing these possibilities requires detailed comparisons of IMF constraints from complementary methods in a sample of \"benchmark\" galaxies with high-quality data.</p><p>Strong-lensing galaxies are particularly useful for this purpose, since with high-resolution imaging the total mass <em>M</em><sub>Ein</sub> projected within the Einstein radius can be measured to 1%\u20132% precision (Treu <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib76\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib76\">2010</a>). Large samples of galaxy-scale lenses at <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a00.2 (Auger et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib2\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib2\">2009</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib3\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib3\">2010a</a>; Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib77\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib77\">2010</a>) have been used to study the IMF, but lower-redshift lenses are especially valuable for two reasons. First, the Einstein radius is typically located at a smaller fraction of the effective radius, where the dark matter fraction is smaller. Second, obtaining spectroscopy with adequate depth and wavelength coverage to constrain the IMF is more practical for nearby lenses.</p><p>Smith &amp; Lucey (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib68\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib68\">2013</a>) investigated ESO325-G004 (also known as SNL-0), then the closest known strong lens, and showed that it does not follow the mean IMF trends described above. Despite being a massive ETG with <em>\u03c3</em>\u00a0~\u00a0300\u00a0km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup> and [Mg/Fe]\u00a0~\u00a0+0.3, its IMF was found to be consistent with that of the Milky Way, with a mass factor <em>\u03b1</em>\u00a0=\u00a01.04\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.15 that is a factor of <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />1.5 smaller than the mean <em>\u03c3</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>\u03b1</em> relations found by Treu et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib77\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib77\">2010</a>), Conroy &amp; van Dokkum (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib21\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib21\">2012b</a>), and Cappellari et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib13c\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib13c\">2013c</a>). Following this initial study, (Smith et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib69\">2015b</a>, hereafter <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib69\">S15</a>) embarked on the SINFONI Nearby Elliptical Lens Locator Survey (SNELLS), which uncovered two new nearby strong lenses at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.03 and 0.05. Surprisingly, these two lenses were also shown to favor a relatively \"lightweight\" IMF. The mean <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816dieqn1.gif\" alt=\"$\\langle \\alpha \\rangle =1.10\\pm 0.08\\pm 0.10$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\langle \\alpha \\rangle =1.10\\pm 0.08\\pm 0.10</script></span></span> (statistical and systematic uncertainties, respectively) for the SNELLS sample is consistent with a Milky Way-like IMF and strongly inconsistent with the \"heavy\" IMFs favored by other studies for typical ETGs of similar <em>\u03c3</em> or [Mg/Fe].</p><p>Do the SNELLS lenses reveal a fundamental inconsistency in different methods used to infer the IMF? Are they indicative of a large scatter in the IMF, perhaps driven by yet unknown parameters? Or can the apparent discrepancies be resolved by relaxing some of the typical assumptions, e.g., allowing more flexibility in the shape of the IMF? In this paper we address these questions by comparing the lensing, dynamical, and spectroscopic methods of IMF estimation for the three SNELLS lenses. We first present lensing and stellar dynamical estimates of the total <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> and stellar <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em>. We then analyze new spectroscopic observations with an updated version of the (Conroy &amp; van Dokkum <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib21\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib21\">2012b</a>, hereafter <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib21\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib21\">CvD12</a>) SPS models and compare these to the lensing and dynamical results.</p><p>Throughout we adopt the distances, effective radii, and Einstein radii of the SNELLS lenses measured by <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib69\">S15</a>.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa816ds2\" name=\"apjaa816ds2\">2.\u00a0Observations</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><h3 id=\"apjaa816ds2-1\" name=\"apjaa816ds2-1\">2.1.\u00a0Magellan/IMACS Spectroscopy</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The SNELLS lenses were observed using the IMACS spectrograph (Dressler et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib26\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib26\">2011</a>) at the 6.5 m Magellan Baade telescope during 2015 April 9\u201310 and 2015 September 25. Spectroscopic observations were made using both the 600/8<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/fdg.gif\" alt=\"fdg\" align=\"baseline\" />6 and 600/13<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/fdg.gif\" alt=\"fdg\" align=\"baseline\" />0 gratings with the <em>f</em>/4 camera in order to cover the wavelength range 3565\u20139415\u00a0\u00c5\u00a0continuously with a uniform resolution of 2.8\u00a0\u00c5. The GG495 filter provided order blocking for the red setup. The 1'' wide long slit was oriented east\u2013west for SNL-0 and SNL-2 and north\u2013south for SNL-1. Total exposure times ranged from 60\u00a0minutes to 100\u00a0minutes per grating.</p><p>Reduction was performed with a combination of IRAF and custom IDL scripts. The two-dimensional wavelength solution was first obtained from arc lamp exposures. This mapping was then used to model the quartz lamp spectrum and slit illumination profile, which were divided from the dome flats to isolate pixel-to-pixel variations. Dome flats at Magellan are obtained using a screen inserted at the pupil. For observations with the red grating, dome flats were interspersed through the observations to mitigate shifts in fringes induced by flexure as the instrument rotates. These proved to be very minimal. The slit illumination was measured using twilight sky exposures. Following flat fielding, cosmic rays were identified and interpolated using <tt>LACOSMIC</tt> (van Dokkum <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib81\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib81\">2001</a>).</p><p>For exposures obtained with the red grating, consecutive dithered images were subtracted to obtain a first-pass sky subtraction. The spectra were then rectified, making small zero-point corrections to the wavelength solution using night sky lines. Residual sky emission in the red setup, and all sky emission in the blue setup, was removed by subtracting the flux averaged within apertures extending 33''\u201344'' from the galaxy center.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa816dfn1\">6</a></sup>\n\n</p><p>For our dynamical analysis, we then registered and averaged the two-dimensional spectra from each exposure. For our stellar population analysis, we extracted one-dimensional spectra from each exposure and averaged them. This extraction was weighted along the spatial axis such that the resulting spectrum mimics one obtained within a circular aperture with a 2<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />2 radius. Specifically, we applied a weight of unity to the central 1''\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a01'' and weighted the outer rows by the ratio of the area of a half-annulus (<em>\u03c0R</em> <b>&#x00d7;</b> <em>dR</em>) to the part subtended by the slit (<em>dR</em> <b>&#x00d7;</b> <em>s</em>), or <em>\u03c0R</em>/<em>s</em>, where <em>s</em>\u00a0=\u00a01'' is the slit width, <em>dR</em>\u00a0=\u00a00<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />22 is the binned pixel scale, and <em>R</em> is the distance to the galaxy center. This 2<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />2 aperture is close to <em>\u03b8</em><sub>Ein</sub> and so enables a direct comparison between lensing and spectroscopic IMF estimates. (We quantify the possible effect of small residual aperture mismatch in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa816ds6-1\">6.1</a>.) This is also the maximum radius for which we could extract a near-infrared spectrum with matched aperture, owing to the shorter slit of the FIRE spectrograph (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa816ds2-2\">2.2</a>).</p><p>The spectra were then divided by a flux calibration curve obtained using a combination of the quartz lamp spectrum, which captures high-frequency features in the instrumental response, and observations of white dwarf standards from Moehler et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib47a\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib47a\">2014</a>). Telluric absorption was removed by fitting synthetic transmission spectra generated by the <tt>molecfit</tt> radiative transfer code (Kausch et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib33\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib33\">2015</a>; Smette et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib65\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib65\">2015</a>). The correction proceeded iteratively. A first-pass SPS model of the galaxy spectrum was fit using <tt>ppxf</tt> (Cappellari &amp; Emsellem <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib11\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib11\">2004</a>) with the regions of strongest telluric absorption masked. Dividing the galaxy spectrum by this model produces an approximate transmission spectrum, which was then fit using <tt>molecfit</tt>. The original galaxy spectrum was then divided by this fitted synthetic transmission spectrum and refit with SPS models (now without additional masking). After three iterations, this process accurately removed telluric absorption over the wavelength range used in our analysis (<em>\u03bb</em><sub>rest</sub> &lt; 8900 \u00c5). Typical rms residuals were only 1.4<b>&#x00d7;</b> the formal noise; these residuals include random noise along with any systematic errors in the telluric absorption correction and the modeling of the galaxy spectrum.</p><p>IMACS disperses onto four detectors, and our spectra therefore have several ~50\u00a0\u00c5\u00a0gaps. The final processing step was to resample the stacked spectra from the multiple detectors and multiple gratings into a single spectrum with 100 km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup> bins. Small velocity corrections were made to ensure that spectra obtained with the blue and red configurations share a common systemic redshift. The median signal-to-noise ratio per 100\u00a0km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup> bin redward of Mg\u00a0b\u00a0was 320 for SNL-0 and SNL-1 and 140 for SNL-2 (equivalently, 210 and 90 per \u00c5, respectively).</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaa816ds2-2\" name=\"apjaa816ds2-2\">2.2.\u00a0Magellan/FIRE Spectroscopy</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>All SNELLS lenses were also observed using FIRE, a near-infrared echellete spectrograph at the Magellan Baade telescope (Simcoe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib64\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib64\">2013</a>), during the nights of 2015 April 8, May 3, and September 25. The FIRE spectra cover 0.82\u20132.51 <em>\u03bc</em>m, but in this paper we use only the region around the Wing\u2013Ford band of FeH near 9916\u00a0\u00c5\u00a0for SNL-0 and SNL-1. Observations of SNL-2 were also made but had an insufficient signal-to-noise ratio and so are excluded from our analysis. On-target exposure times for SNL-0 and SNL-1 were 32\u00a0minutes and 54\u00a0minutes, respectively. We operated FIRE in the up-the-ramp sampling mode. The 1'' wide slit provided a resolution of <em>R</em>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a04000.</p><p>The short 6'' FIRE slit does not permit sky subtraction within the science exposures. Instead, we nodded the telescope 2' to obtain a blank sky spectrum every 4\u00a0minutes. Initial reduction steps, including wavelength calibration and flat fielding, were performed with the FIREHOSE pipeline. Remaining steps were performed with custom IDL routines. We first modeled and subtracted the background measured between orders, in order to remove scattered light and amplifier offsets. After rectifying the images and improving the wavelength calibration, the sky exposure associated with each sky frame was then modestly rescaled and shifted in wavelength to minimize sky line residuals in each order. Spectra were then extracted in each order, using the same aperture and weighting as the IMACS spectra, and flux calibrated using a white dwarf standard. Orders were finally combined, allowing for small residual flux offsets between orders determined using the regions of overlap. Telluric absorption was removed as described in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa816ds2-1\">2.1</a>.</p><p>We found that the continuum shape varied somewhat in observations made on different nights. Before combining the one-dimensional spectra from multiple nights, we normalized the spectra in the rest-frame 9600\u201310200\u00a0\u00c5\u00a0range using a cubic polynomial and resampled to 100 km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup> bins. The final signal-to-noise ratio per 100\u00a0km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup> bin at the Wing\u2013Ford band is 210 for SNL-0 and 320 for SNL-1 (equivalently, 110 and 170 per \u00c5, respectively).</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaa816ds2-3\" name=\"apjaa816ds2-3\">2.3.\u00a0VLT/X-shooter Spectroscopy</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We acquired optical and near-infrared spectra for all the SNELLS lenses with X-shooter (Vernet et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib84\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib84\">2011</a>) at the 8.2 m UT2 of the ESO Very Large Telescope (VLT). The observations were made using the image-slicing integral field unit (IFU), providing a 4.0\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a01.8 arcsec<sup>2</sup> aperture, oriented close to the parallactic angle. For each galaxy the data comprise three on-source and two off-source sky exposures (40'' distant) observed in an ABABA sequence.</p><p>Two dichroics in X-shooter divide the light among three arms. The analyses reported in this paper use only the UVB and VIS data, for which the total exposure time was 23\u00a0minutes. The data were processed using the standard X-shooter pipeline to generate five separate data cubes, one for each echelle order, from the exposures. Subsequently, we combined the object and sky exposures for each order, and used the ESO <tt>skycorr</tt> (Noll et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib53\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib53\">2014</a>) and <tt>molecfit</tt> tools to account for sky variation between the exposures, and to derive corrections for telluric absorption. These steps were performed separately for each of the three IFU slices, to help account for small shifts in the wavelength solution residuals between the slices. The echelle orders were matched using low-order polynomial corrections derived through comparison to a nominal SSP model spectrum. Spectra were extracted by summing the flux within the entire IFU field. Outlying flux points were identified at the original high spectral resolution (<em>R</em> \u2248 8000) before resampling the combined spectra onto 100 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> velocity bins.</p><p>In comparisons of the X-shooter spectra with the IMACS data, SPS models, and Sloan Digital Sky Survey (SDSS) composite spectra, we noticed a significant \"ringing\" near the wavelength of the UVB-VIS dichroic, which could not be removed by normal data reduction steps. The ringing probably arises from the complex instrument response around the dichroic and its temporal variation (e.g., Sch\u00f6nebeck et al .<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib62\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib62\">2014</a>).</p><p>Because of this difficulty, we prefer the IMACS spectra for our main SPS analysis in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa816ds6\">6</a>. We use the X-shooter data for two purposes: first, to measure the aperture velocity dispersion <em>\u03c3</em><sub><em>e</em>/2</sub> in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa816ds4-4\">4.4</a>, and second, to fill in a small but important gap in the IMACS spectral coverage around <em>\u03bb</em><sub>rest</sub>\u00a0\u2248\u00a04160\u20134260 \u00c5. This region contains the Ca4227 feature, which is important to constrain [Ca/Fe]. Specifically, we match the line width (including instrumental resolution and galaxy velocity dispersion), redshift, and continuum shape of the X-shooter spectrum to those of the IMACS spectrum in the vicinity of this gap and then insert the matched spectrum. This procedure assumes that the IMACS and X-shooter apertures probe a similar mean radius within the galaxy. The mean light-weighted radius within the IFU field of view is approximately equal to that within a circular aperture having <em>R</em>\u00a0=\u00a01<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />6. Since this is fairly close to our <em>R</em>\u00a0=\u00a02<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />2 IMACS aperture, we consider that the effect of any abundance gradients will be minimal.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaa816ds2-4\" name=\"apjaa816ds2-4\">2.4.\u00a0Imaging</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We obtained <em>r</em>-band images of SNL-1 and SNL-2 using the LDSS-3 imaging spectrograph at the Magellan 2 telescope in photometric conditions. Photometric calibration was tied to the SDSS DR9 (Ahn et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib1\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib1\">2012</a>) using observations of stars in Stripe 82. Night-to-night variations indicate a conservative zero-point uncertainty of 3%. For SNL-0, we used Hubble Heritage observations taken with the Advanced Camera for Surveys and the F625W filter (Proposal 10710). When constructing our dynamical model of SNL-2, we also use an <em>R</em>-band image obtained in excellent seeing with FORS2 at the VLT. Since these data were not obtained in photometric conditions, we calibrated them by matching the luminosity within <em>\u03b8</em><sub>Ein</sub> to the LDSS-3 measurement (see Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa816ds3\">3</a>).</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa816ds3\" name=\"apjaa816ds3\">3.\u00a0Lensing <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> and <em>M<sub><img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lowast.gif\" alt=\"lowast\" align=\"baseline\" /></sub></em>/<em>L</em></h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>\n<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib69\">S15</a> measured the luminosities <em>L</em><sub>Ein</sub> of the SNELLS lenses within their Einstein radii <em>\u03b8</em><sub>Ein</sub> in the <em>J</em> band. We have remeasured <em>L</em><sub>Ein</sub> in the SDSS <em>r</em> band in order to facilitate comparisons with other studies. We used the images introduced in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa816ds2-4\">2.4</a> to measure the flux within a circular aperture with radius <em>\u03b8</em><sub>Ein</sub> for each lens. The raw flux was then corrected for bandpass shifting and filter differences (0\u20130.06 mag), estimated using FSPS (Conroy et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib19\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib19\">2009</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib23\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib23\">2010</a>; Conroy &amp; Gunn <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib18\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib18\">2010</a>); Galactic extinction (<em>A</em><sub><em>r</em></sub> = 0.05\u20130.13 mag; Schlafly &amp; Finkbeiner <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib61\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib61\">2011</a>); and the point-spread function (0\u20130.07 mag) following <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib69\">S15</a>. The derived luminosities are listed in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa816dt1\">1</a>, with 2%\u20133% uncertainties estimated from photometric zero-point and Galactic extinction errors. Also listed are the lensing-based total <em>M</em>/<em>L</em><sub><em>r</em></sub> based on the masses <em>M</em><sub>Ein</sub> found by <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib69\">S15</a>. The lensing <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> is proportional to distance, which was derived from the Hubble flow, and we include an uncertainty corresponding to a 500\u00a0km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup> peculiar velocity (3%\u20134%).</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apjaa816dt1\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table 1.</b>\u00a0\nMeasured Quantities for SNELLS Lenses\n</p><table xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub></td>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\"><em>\u03b1</em><sub>2PL+cut</sub></td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" type=\"math/tex\">{1.58}_{-0.18}^{+0.20}</script></span></span>\n</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">\n<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span type=\"math/tex\">{1.66}_{-0.24}^{+0.27}</script></span></span>\n</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">\n<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span type=\"math/tex\">{1.05}_{-0.20}^{+0.26}</script></span></span>\n</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">Double power-law with low-mass cutoff</td>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\"><em>\u03b1</em><sub>non-p</sub></td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">(<em>M</em>/<em>L</em>)<sub>\u2299,<em>r</em></sub></td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">\n<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816dieqn21.gif\" alt=\"${1.54}_{-0.25}^{+0.28}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{1.54}_{-0.25}^{+0.28}</script></span></span>\n</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">1.41\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.15</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">1.04\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.17</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">Nonparametric IMF</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody></table><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/845/2/157/suppdata/apjaa816dt1_ascii.txt?doi=10.3847/1538-4357/aa816d\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/845/2/157/suppdata/apjaa816dt1_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>SNL-2 has a neighboring galaxy separated by 7'' that could plausibly contribute to <em>L</em><sub>Ein</sub>. By fitting two-dimensional S\u00e9rsic models to the galaxies with <tt>galfit</tt> (Peng et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib55\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib55\">2002</a>), we estimate that this contribution is only 1.5%, a minor effect that we neglect.</p><p>Proceeding from the total <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> to the stellar <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em> requires an estimate of the dark matter contribution within <em>\u03b8</em><sub>Ein</sub>. This cannot be estimated from the lensing constraints alone. One route is to appeal to simulations of galaxy formation. Here we follow <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib69\">S15</a>, who used the EAGLE simulations (Schaller et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib58\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib58\">2015</a>; Schaye et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib59\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib59\">2015</a>) to estimate dark matter contributions that range from 16% and 25% of <em>M</em><sub>Ein</sub>. Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa816dt1\">1</a> lists the stellar (<em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em><sub><em>r</em></sub>)<sub>L+EAGLE</sub> derived from lensing and the EAGLE simulations. In the following section, we present an alternate method of estimating the dark matter contribution.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa816ds4\" name=\"apjaa816ds4\">4.\u00a0Dynamical <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> and <em>M<sub>*</sub></em>/<em>L</em></h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>In addition to their utility for modeling the stellar populations of the SNELLS lenses, the IMACS spectra provide the opportunity to measure dynamical <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> and to estimate the stellar <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em> through a joint lensing+dynamics analysis. Here we describe our kinematic measurements and the dynamical modeling.</p><h3 id=\"apjaa816ds4-1\" name=\"apjaa816ds4-1\">4.1.\u00a0Stellar Kinematics</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Resolved stellar kinematics were measured using IMACS long-slit spectra. We preferred the IMACS data for this purpose since they cover a larger radial extent than the X-shooter IFU. Spectra were extracted from the combined two-dimensional spectrum for each lens, with the bin size adjusted to reach a minimum signal-to-noise ratio. We used our model fit to the integrated spectrum, described in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa816ds6\">6</a>, as the template. This template is redshifted, broadened by a Gaussian line-of-sight velocity distribution, multiplied by a 5th-degree polynomial, and added to a 14th-degree polynomial to fit each bin using the <tt>ppxf</tt> code. The procedure accounts for the instrumental and template resolutions.</p><p>Reasonable variations in the polynomial order affect the derived <em>\u03c3</em> by <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />3%, which was added in quadrature to the random errors. Additionally, templates obtained using stars from the MILES library (S\u00e1nchez-Bl\u00e1zquez et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib57\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib57\">2006</a>) or the Vazdekis et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib83\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib83\">2012</a>) SPS models lead to dispersions that are uniformly larger than our default values by up to 5%, which we account for below as a correlated systematic uncertainty. This is likely a conservative estimate of the uncertainty, given that our default template produces a significantly lower <em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup> than the others.</p><p>The derived kinematic profiles are shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa816df1\">1</a>. SNL-0 and SNL-2 are entirely pressure supported with no detectable rotation. The situation is different for SNL-1, where rotational support is significant. The IMACS long-slit data do not sample the the velocity field azimuthally, and the X-shooter IFU data are not radially extended enough to constrain the more complicated dynamical structure of SNL-1. For these reasons, we exclude SNL-1 from the dynamical analysis and focus on SNL-0 and SNL-2.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa816df1\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa816df1\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816df1_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 1.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 1.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816df1_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 1.</strong>\u00a0Left: isophotal contours (red) are overlaid on the images of each SNELLS lens introduced in Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa816ds2-4\">2.4</a>. Blue contours show the corresponding isophotes of the multi-Gaussian expansion used in our dynamical modeling. Black lines show the position of the IMACS slit. North is up and east is left; axis units are in arcsec. Right: observed stellar kinematics along the IMACS slit are shown with the JAM fits overlaid for SNL-0 and SNL-2 (see Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa816ds4-2\">4.2</a>). The solid line is the median posterior model in fits to the dynamics only; the dotted line refers to fits that also include <em>M</em><sub>Ein</sub> as a constraint. Note the different axis scales for <span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816dieqn22.gif\" alt=\"$\\sqrt{{\\sigma }^{2}+{v}^{2}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\sqrt{{\\sigma }^{2}+{v}^{2}}</script></span></span> and <em>v</em>. Error bars on <em>\u03c3</em> do not include an overall 5% systematic uncertainty. Vertical lines indicate the location of the Einstein radius.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaa816df1\" href=\"/0004-637X/845/2/157/downloadFigure/figure/apjaa816df1\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaa816df1\" href=\"/0004-637X/845/2/157/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaa816df1\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div><h3 id=\"apjaa816ds4-2\" name=\"apjaa816ds4-2\">4.2.\u00a0M/L from Dynamics Only</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Stellar dynamics were calculated using Jeans anisotropic modeling (JAM; Cappellari <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib10\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib10\">2008</a>). JAM requires that the projected luminosity density be expressed via multi-Gaussian expansions, which we show in the left panels of Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa816df1\">1</a>. SNL-0 and SNL-1 are accurately described by these expansions. Although SNL-2 has more complex isophotes near its neighboring galaxy, which is modeled and subtracted in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa816df1\">1</a>, it too is well described by the model within the <em>R</em>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a03'' extent of our kinematic constraints.</p><p>Our fiducial mass model consists of three components: (1) the stellar mass traced by the the <em>r</em>-band luminosity, (2) a dark matter halo, and (3) a black hole on the McConnell &amp; Ma (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib47\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib47\">2013</a>) <em>M</em><sub>BH</sub>\u2013<em>\u03c3</em> relation. Given the nearly round (<em>b</em>/<em>a</em>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a00.8) isophotes of SNL-0 and SNL-2, by default we use spherical dynamical models. We include a radially invariant velocity anisotropy parameter <em>\u03b2</em><sub><em>r</em></sub> and adopt a prior <em>\u03b2</em><sub><em>r</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a00\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.3 that encodes previous findings (e.g., Cappellari et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib12\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib12\">2007</a>) that the central stellar kinematics are not very far from isotropic in massive ellipticals. The halo is parameterized with a generalized NFW (i.e., a gNFW) dark matter halo (see Equation\u00a0(2) of Newman et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib51\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib51\">2013a</a>). The scale radius is fixed to <em>r</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a030 kpc, following Treu et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib77\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib77\">2010</a>) and Posacki et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib56\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib56\">2015</a>). Our constraints are located well within <em>r</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>, so the density profile is effectively a power law <em>\u03c1</em><sub>DM</sub>\u00a0\u221d\u00a0<em>r</em><sup>\u2212<em>\u03b1</em></sup>. The NFW profile has <em>\u03b1</em><sub>DM</sub>\u00a0=\u00a01, but it may be modified by the growth of the stellar component. The dark matter density slope in galaxy-scale lenses is not well known, and empirical estimates range from <em>\u03b1</em><sub>DM</sub>\u00a0=\u00a01.0\u20131.7 (see Newman et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib50\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib50\">2015</a>, Figure 15). We therefore adopt a uniform prior on <em>\u03b1</em><sub>DM</sub> over this range. We parameterize the normalization using the fraction <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub> of dark matter within a cylinder of radius <em>\u03b8</em><sub>Ein</sub> and take a uniform prior over [0, 1]. The free parameters are then <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em>, <em>\u03b2</em><sub><em>r</em></sub>, <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub> and <em>\u03b1</em><sub>DM</sub>.</p><p>For a given combination of parameters, we generate a map of the projected <em>\u03c3</em>. This map is blurred by the 1'' seeing and binned like the data. Since we are using spherical dynamical models, we circularize the radii of the observations.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa816dfn2\">7</a></sup>\n The parameter space is explored using Markov chain Monte Carlo (MCMC) sampling.</p><p>To compare to the lensing masses, we computed the total <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> for our model, including all mass components, within a cylinder having a radius <em>\u03b8</em><sub>Ein</sub>. We included the distance uncertainty and a 5% correlated systematic uncertainty in the <em>\u03c3</em> measurements. Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa816df1\">1</a> shows that the JAM models accurately fit the long-slit kinematics.
