Compared to the total lensing masses within the same aperture, we find that the dynamical estimates of <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> are <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />16% higher for both SNL-0 and SNL-2 at a significance of <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />1<em>\u03c3</em> (see Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa816dt1\">1</a>).</p><p>With the present constraints, consisting only of long-slit kinematics and no higher-order moments of the line-of-sight velocity distribution, there is a degeneracy between <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> and <em>\u03b2</em><sub><em>r</em></sub>. When fitting the stellar kinematics alone, we find <em>\u03b2</em><sub><em>r</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.17\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.13 and 0.13\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.19 for SNL-0 and SNL-2, respectively. As we show below, with higher values of <em>\u03b2</em><sub><em>r</em></sub> , it is possible to adequately fit the lensing and dynamics constraints jointly.</p><p>In order to assess systematic uncertainties, we varied several of our model assumptions and inputs to estimate the effect on <em>M</em>/<em>L</em>. First, to test for possible gradients in <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> or <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em> that may be not well described by our mass model, we restricted the velocity dispersion constraints to those within <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816dieqn23.gif\" alt=\"${\\theta }_{\\mathrm{Ein}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\theta }_{\\mathrm{Ein}}</script></span></span>. This led to negligible variations of <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />5% in <em>M</em>/<em>L</em>. Therefore, the dynamical and lensing total <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> refer to apertures that are effectively matched. Second, we considered simpler single-component models in which mass follows light. This led to no change in <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> for SNL-2 and a small increase of 6% for SNL-0. Third, we varied the mass model components by (1) fixing the dark matter halo to the NFW slope <em>\u03b1</em><sub>DM</sub>\u00a0=\u00a01, or (2) including no black hole or a 10<sup>10</sup> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> black hole, or (3) considering axisymmetric dynamical models, in which the velocity dispersion ellipsoid is fixed in cylindrical coordinates, rather than the spherical coordinates of our default models. Combinations of these changes produced an <em>M</em>/<em>L</em><sub><em>r</em></sub> in the range 5.8\u20136.2 for SNL-0 and 5.1\u20136.0 for SNL-2, compared to 5.9\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.7 and 5.6\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.7 for the fiducial models, respectively. Thus, the effect of these model variations is comparable to the uncertainty we assigned to the fiducial <em>M</em>/<em>L</em>.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaa816ds4-3\" name=\"apjaa816ds4-3\">4.3.\u00a0M<sub>*</sub>/L from Lensing and Dynamics Combined</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>In addition to the total <em>M</em>/<em>L</em>, the mass modeling procedure outlined in the previous section allows us to constrain <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em>. This ability to separate the dark and stellar components rests on our chosen prior on the dark matter density profile slope described in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa816ds4-2\">4.2</a>. Although the separation can, in principle, be done with kinematic constraints alone, the addition of lensing information can help break the degeneracy with velocity anisotropy. Following earlier work on intermediate-redshift lenses (e.g., Auger et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib4\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib4\">2010b</a>; Spiniello et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib72\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib72\">2011</a>; Barnab\u00e8 et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib5\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib5\">2013</a>; Newman et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib50\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib50\">2015</a>), we jointly modeled the stellar kinematics and lensing. We did this using the same mass models as described in the previous section, but we now imposed the projected mass <em>M</em><sub>Ein</sub> within the Einstein radius (see <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib69\">S15</a>) as an additional constraint. This provides a different route to <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em> from the lensing and EAGLE simulation-based constraints in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa816ds3\">3</a>.</p><p>Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa816df1\">1</a> shows that the joint models are able to fit the kinematics accurately; the <em>M</em><sub>Ein</sub> constraint is also satisfied within 1<em>\u03c3</em>. We infer more radially biased orbits in the joint analysis: <em>\u03b2</em><sub><em>r</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.4\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.1 and 0.2\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.2 for SNL-0 and SNL-2, respectively. The former value is at the top end of the range reported for massive ellipticals (e.g., Gerhard et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib27\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib27\">2001</a>; Cappellari et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib12\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib12\">2007</a>). However, <em>\u03b2</em><sub><em>r</em></sub> is very sensitive to the black hole mass and could be much smaller; for example, we would find <em>\u03b2</em><sub><em>r</em></sub>\u00a0\u2248\u00a00.1 for SNL-0 if a 10<sup>10</sup> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> black hole were present.</p><p>Constraints on (<em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em><sub><em>r</em></sub>)<sub>L+D</sub>, marginalized over other parameters, are given in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa816dt1\">1</a> for the fiducial model: 4.1\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.9 and 4.9\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.6 for SNL-0 and SNL-2, respectively. These errors include the distance uncertainty and a 5% correlated uncertainty in the velocity dispersions. We estimated dark matter fractions within the Einstein radius of <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.23\u00b1\u00a00.10 for SNL-0 and <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.12 (68% confidence) for SNL-2. The former is consistent with the EAGLE simulation-based estimate used by <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib69\">S15</a>, while the latter is lower.</p><p>To test the sensitivity of <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em> to the modeling assumptions, we repeated the tests described in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa816ds4-2\">4.2</a>. For our default spherical dynamical models, the data do not constrain the black hole mass <em>M</em><sub>BH</sub>. If we assume that a massive 10<sup>10</sup> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> black hole is present, then <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em> is reduced from our fiducial models by 3% and 20% for SNL-0 and SNL-2, respectively. For the oblate axisymmetric dynamical models, <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em> is much more sensitive to <em>M</em><sub>BH</sub>. If we introduce <em>M</em><sub>BH</sub> as a free parameter, axisymmetric models for both SNL-0 and SNL-2 favor very massive black holes (<em>M</em><sub>BH</sub>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a02 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>10</sup> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>) and low <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em> (30%\u201340% below the fiducial values). Such a situation is possible but unlikely, and we consider that more detailed kinematic data with higher spatial resolution and two-dimensional information are required to derive a meaningful constraint on <em>M</em><sub>BH</sub>. Nonetheless, this exercise shows that <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em> is more sensitive than the total <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> to the black hole mass and the parameterization of the velocity dispersion ellipsoid. Importantly, the variations to our fiducial dynamical model described above would mainly <em>reduce</em> the inferred <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em>.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaa816ds4-4\" name=\"apjaa816ds4-4\">4.4.\u00a0Aperture Velocity Dispersion <em>\u03c3</em><sub><em>e</em>/2</sub></h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>In order to examine trends among ETGs, it is also useful to measure a velocity dispersion within a standardized aperture. We used a circular aperture with radius <em>R</em><sub><em>e</em></sub>/2. Velocity dispersions <em>\u03c3</em><sub>raw</sub> were measured from the X-shooter spectra over <em>\u03bb</em><sub>rest</sub>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a03700\u20138700\u00a0\u00c5, which we preferred for this purpose since the IFU more completely samples the azimuthal distribution, which is complex for SNL-1, than do the long-slit IMACS data. We assumed that <em>\u03c3</em><sub>raw</sub> probes an effective circular aperture with area equal to the rectangular IFU field. The measured dispersions were then standardized to the <em>R</em><sub><em>e</em></sub>/2 aperture using the scaling <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816dieqn24.gif\" alt=\"$\\sigma (R\\lt {R}_{0})\\propto {R}_{0}^{-0.06}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\sigma (R\\lt {R}_{0})\\propto {R}_{0}^{-0.06}</script></span></span> that we derived from high-<em>\u03c3</em> galaxies in the ATLAS<sup>3D</sup>\u00a0survey (Cappellari et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib13\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib13\">2013a</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib14\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib14\">2013b</a>). The aperture-corrected <em>\u03c3</em><sub><em>e</em>/2</sub>\u00a0=\u00a0<em>\u03c3</em>(<em>R</em> &lt; <em>R</em><sub><em>e</em></sub>/2) are listed in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa816dt1\">1</a>. Although the formal uncertainties are only 1\u20132\u00a0km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup>, we conservatively assigned 5% errors based on the template tests described in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa816ds4-2\">4.2</a>.</p><p>Compared to the velocity dispersions cataloged by the 6dF survey (Campbell et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib9\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib9\">2014</a>) and used by <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib69\">S15</a>, the present values are systematically lower, even after accounting for aperture differences. This difference probably arises, at least in part, from the Eddington bias: the SNELLS targets were selected to have <em>\u03c3</em><sub>6dF</sub>\u00a0&gt;\u00a0300 km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup>, and owing to the steep velocity dispersion function and noisier 6dF data, random scattering will result in biased velocity dispersions for the highest-<em>\u03c3</em><sub>6dF</sub> sources. We show in the Appendix that the highest-<em>\u03c3</em><sub>6dF</sub> galaxies indeed have 6dF velocity dispersions that are systematically higher than other independent measurements in the literature. We conclude that the discrepancy between our new velocity dispersions and the 6dF values reflects a bias in the 6dF catalog when selecting galaxies in the extreme high-<em>\u03c3</em><sub>6dF</sub> tail of the distribution (see the Appendix).</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa816ds5\" name=\"apjaa816ds5\">5.\u00a0Results: Lensing and Dynamical Masses and IMF Constraints</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>With estimates of <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> and <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em> derived from our lensing and dynamical analyses, we now consider the SNELLS galaxies in the context of the ETG population and discuss the resulting constraints on their IMF, before comparing these to constraints from SPS models in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa816ds6\">6</a>.</p><h3 id=\"apjaa816ds5-1\" name=\"apjaa816ds5-1\">5.1.\u00a0Total M/L</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>As mentioned in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa816ds4-2\">4.2</a>, the total <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> values derived from dynamics alone are 16% higher than the lensing values for SNL-0 and SNL-2. Although the dynamical values are higher in both cases, which may suggest a systematic difference in the mass estimates, the <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />1<em>\u03c3</em> differences in <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> are also compatible within the uncertainties. Another potential source of error is the luminosity. Since the lensing and dynamical analyses rely on the same images, any error in the photometric calibration would affect both measurements. As we show below, we obtain lensing-based IMF constraints that are extremely close to those of <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib69\">S15</a>. Since these analyses use the same masses but different images to derive <em>L</em><sub>Ein</sub>, this rules out significant errors in the luminosities.</p><p>Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa816df2\">2</a> also presents a comparison between the SNELLS sample and the general ETG population. Even before proceeding to the decomposition of dark matter and stars or constraints on the IMF, it is clear that the SNELLS lenses have a uniformly low <em>total M</em>/<em>L</em> for their <em>\u03c3</em>, as compared to relations derived from the ATLAS<sup>3D</sup>\u00a0and SLACS surveys. This is true for both lensing and dynamical estimates, and it suggests the influence of some selection effect in the SNELLS sample. The presence of strong lensing largely depends on <em>\u03c3</em> with a cross section \u221d<em>\u03c3</em><sup>4</sup>, so a pure lensing selection should not bias <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> at a given <em>\u03c3</em>. Studies of the SLACS sample have shown that lenses are not distinguishable from non-lensing ETGs in their photometric, dynamical, or environmental properties, once the samples are matched in <em>\u03c3</em> (Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib79\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib79\">2006</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib78\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib78\">2009</a>; Bolton et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib7\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib7\">2008a</a>). Indeed, lensing (SLACS) and non-lensing (ATLAS<sup>3D</sup>) samples define very consistent <em>\u03c3</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>M</em>/<em>L</em> relations, and the SNELLS lenses lie below both (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa816df2\">2</a>, and see Posacki et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib56\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib56\">2015</a>). The lensing properties of the SNELLS galaxies are also not unusual: <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816dieqn25.gif\" alt=\"$\\langle {\\sigma }_{e/2}/{\\sigma }_{\\mathrm{SIE}}\\rangle =0.97$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\langle {\\sigma }_{e/2}/{\\sigma }_{\\mathrm{SIE}}\\rangle =0.97</script></span></span>, consistent with the SLACS lenses. Since this quantity maps closely to the mass density profile slope (Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib78\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib78\">2009</a>), the mass structure of the SNELLS lenses is also typical.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa816df2\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa816df2\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816df2_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 2.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 2.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816df2_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 2.</strong>\u00a0Top panel: total <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> of the SNELLS lenses, inferred from lensing or dynamics, compared to the ATLAS<sup>3D</sup>\u00a0(Cappellari et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib13\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib13\">2013a</a>, <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib14\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib14\">2013b</a>) and SLACS samples (Auger et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib2\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib2\">2009</a>, <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib3\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib3\">2010a</a>) of ETGs. For the latter, we use the <em>M</em>/<em>L</em><sub><em>V</em></sub> evolved to <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00 by Auger et al.\u00a0and convert them into <em>r</em> band assuming a galaxy color <em>V</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>r</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.34. (This color corresponds to a 10 Gyr old population with [<em>Z</em>/H] = +0.2 in the FSPS models.) Linear fits from Cappellari et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib14\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib14\">2013b</a>) and Auger et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib3\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib3\">2010a</a>) and their 1<em>\u03c3</em> scatter are overlaid. Middle panel: stellar <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em> of the SNELLS lenses, derived from lensing/dynamics, compared to the same samples and to SPS results from <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib21\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib21\">CvD12</a>. The SNELLS results are shown with dark matter contributions from the EAGLE simulations (\"L+EAGLE\") or from joint lensing and dynamical modeling (\"L+D\"). For the SLACS points, we use <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em><sub><em>r</em></sub> from Posacki et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib56\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib56\">2015</a>) and approximate the luminosity evolution to <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00 using the <em>V</em>-band evolution from Auger et al. Bottom panel: the IMF mass factor <em>\u03b1</em> (Equation (<a class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaa816deqn1\">1</a>)) is compared among the same samples. The SLACS results, and the linear relation from the combined ATLAS<sup>3D</sup> and SLACS samples, are from Posacki et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib56\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib56\">2015</a>), with their reported <em>\u03b1</em> multiplied by 1.53 to convert from a Salpeter into a Kroupa convention; this factor is derived from the Vazdekis et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib83\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib83\">2012</a>) models used by Posacki et al. Note that the SNELLS lenses have low <em>M</em>/<em>L</em>, <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em>, and <em>\u03b1</em>, as estimated from lensing/dynamics, compared to the mean ETG of equal <em>\u03c3</em>.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaa816df2\" href=\"/0004-637X/845/2/157/downloadFigure/figure/apjaa816df2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaa816df2\" href=\"/0004-637X/845/2/157/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaa816df2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>Although lenses may not have distinct <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> from the ETG population at fixed <em>\u03c3</em>, a separate possibility is a selection effect in the SNELLS survey. There are two obvious candidates. First, as discussed by <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib69\">S15</a>, the field of view of SINFONI restricts the range of <em>\u03b8</em><sub>Ein</sub> over which multiple images are detectable. Since <em>\u03c3</em><sup>2</sup> is proportional to <em>\u03b8</em><sub>Ein</sub> with only 15% intrinsic scatter (for given source and lens redshifts), and residuals do not correlate with <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> based on studies of the SLACS sample (Bolton et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib8\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib8\">2008b</a>), the field of view may affect the <em>\u03c3</em> distribution of the SNELLS lenses but should not introduce any significant bias in <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> at fixed <em>\u03c3</em>. Second, the surface brightness of the lens could conceivably affect the sensitivity to background sources. However, the SNELLS lenses do not have systematically different surface brightnesses, luminosities, or sizes than the parent 6dF sample (see <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib69\">S15</a>).<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa816dfn3\">8</a></sup>\n\n</p><p>Thus, we have not been able to identify a likely selection effect that would favor low-<em>M</em>/<em>L</em> systems in the SNELLS sample. One must bear in mind that in this respect, the SNELLS lenses are not fully representative of high-<em>\u03c3</em> ETGs. Nevertheless, they provide an excellent opportunity to test the <em>consistency</em> of IMF constraints derived by different methods.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaa816ds5-2\" name=\"apjaa816ds5-2\">5.2.\u00a0Stellar M<sub>*</sub>/L and IMF Constraints</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The lensing and dynamical data place integral constraints on the IMF via the mass factor <em>\u03b1</em>, also referred to as the \"IMF mismatch\" factor:</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaa816deqn1\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816deqn1.gif\" alt=\"Equation (1)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&\\alpha =\\displaystyle \\frac{{M}_{* }/{L}_{r}}{{({M}_{* }/{L}_{r})}_{\\mathrm{MW}}},\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t1\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816dieqn27.gif\" alt=\"${({M}_{* }/{L}_{r})}_{\\mathrm{MW}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{({M}_{* }/{L}_{r})}_{\\mathrm{MW}}</script></span></span> is inferred from SPS modeling assuming a fiducial Kroupa (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib34\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib34\">2001</a>) IMF. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib69\">S15</a> assumed 10\u00a0\u00b1\u00a01\u00a0Gyr ages for the SNELLS lenses in their default results. In a separate model, they instead fit the 6dF spectra to measure (<em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em><sub><em>r</em></sub>)<sub>MW</sub> for each lens, and the resulting ~17% uncertainty was larger than that in <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em><sub><em>r</em></sub> and would dominate the uncertainty in <em>\u03b1</em>.</p><p>We fit our higher quality IMACS spectra using the SPS models described in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa816ds6\">6</a>. The SNELLS lenses span a narrow range of (<em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em><sub><em>r</em></sub>)<sub>MW</sub>\u00a0=\u00a03.7\u20134.0 (see Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa816dt1\">1</a>). Since our spectra now constrain (<em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em><sub><em>r</em></sub>)<sub>MW</sub> to 2%\u20137%, its uncertainty is not a dominant contributor to the error budget for <em>\u03b1</em>, so there is no longer any need to rely on the assumption of old ages.</p><p>Using the lensing mass and EAGLE dark matter contribution, we find <em>\u03b1</em><sub>L+EAGLE</sub>\u00a0=\u00a01.05\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.09, 1.18\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.12, and 0.96\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.14 for the three SNELLS lenses, respectively (Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa816dt1\">1</a>). Since these values are mutually consistent, we determine an average by multiplying the probability distributions: <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816dieqn28.gif\" alt=\"$\\langle {\\alpha }_{{\\rm{L}}+\\mathrm{EAGLE}}\\rangle =1.07\\pm 0.06$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\langle {\\alpha }_{{\\rm{L}}+\\mathrm{EAGLE}}\\rangle =1.07\\pm 0.06</script></span></span>, in agreement with the default <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib69\">S15</a> result, but with the random uncertainties reduced due to the more precise (<em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em><sub><em>r</em></sub>)<sub>MW</sub>. Such a \"lightweight\" IMF is well below the trends shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa816df2\">2</a>, as demonstrated by <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib69\">S15</a>.</p><p>We have also estimated the stellar <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em> and dark matter fraction via a joint lensing+dynamics analysis for SNL-0 and SNL-2. The <em>\u03b1</em> inferred with this method is quite consistent with the lensing+EAGLE estimate for SNL-0 (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa816df2\">2</a> and Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa816dt1\">1</a>). However, as discussed in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa816ds4-2\">4.2</a>, the lensing+dynamics analysis favors a lower dark matter fraction for SNL-2. This increases <em>\u03b1</em> by 40% compared to the lensing+EAGLE estimate and places SNL-2 closer to the mean trend of <em>\u03b1</em> with velocity dispersion (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa816df2\">2</a>, lower panel). Thus, the lensing+dynamics method indicates that the low <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> of SNL-0 arises from a lighter IMF compared to the typical ETG with matched <em>\u03c3</em>, whereas a lower dark matter fraction may partially contribute in SNL-2.</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa816ds6\" name=\"apjaa816ds6\">6.\u00a0Results: Spectroscopic IMF Constraints</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>With constraints on the IMF mass factor <em>\u03b1</em> now in hand from lensing and stellar dynamics, we consider the results obtained from SPS modeling. We use models that are descended from those presented by <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib21\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib21\">CvD12</a>, but which have undergone major changes over the intervening period. These include the use of new isochrones from the MIST project (Choi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib16\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib16\">2016</a>), a new spectral library based on observations from IRTF (Villaume et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib85\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib85\">2017</a>), and new age- and metallicity-dependent response functions. The new models span a significantly wider range in age (1\u201313.5 Gyr) and metallicity (\u22121.5 &lt; [<em>Z</em>/H] &lt; +0.25).</p><p>For the SNELLS analysis, we fit single-age, mono-abundance models with parameters describing (1) the age, redshift, velocity dispersion, and IMF, (2) the abundances of C, N, O(=Ne, S), Na, Mg, Si, K, Ca, Ti, V, Cr, Mn, Fe, Co, Ni, Cu, Sr, Ba, and Eu, (3) nebular emission lines from the Balmer series, [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>], [N <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>], [S <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>], and [N <span class=\"small-caps\">i</span>] with a common redshift and velocity dispersion independent of the stars, (4) contributions from additional hot star light and a \"frosting\" of young stars (0.5\u20133 Gyr), and (5) residual sky emission and absorption, and a constant rescaling of the error spectrum. We do not include nuisance parameters representing additional M giant light or isochrone temperature shifts, unlike <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib21\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib21\">CvD12</a>. Further details are provided by Conroy et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib22\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib22\">2017</a>).</p><p>The assumed shape of the IMF can have a significant effect on the derived <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em>. To explore this dependence, we have constructed and fit models with four parameterizations. In all cases we assumed a slope <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816dieqn29.gif\" alt=\"$\\xi \\propto {m}^{-2.35}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\xi \\propto {m}^{-2.35}</script></span></span> for masses &gt;1 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>. In order of generality, the IMF parameterizations at lower masses are as follows.</p><ul class=\"clear-list wd-inline-list\"><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">1.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">A single power-law over 0.08 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>\u20131 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">2.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">A double power-law over 0.08 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>\u20131 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> with a break at 0.5 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">3.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">A double power-law as in (2), but with a truncation mass allowed to vary from <em>m</em><sub>cut</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.08\u20130.4 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">4.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">A nonparametric form in which the IMF weights are interpolated among four bins distributed between 0.08 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> and 1 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>, as described by Conroy et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib22\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib22\">2017</a>).</div></li></ul><p>\n</p><p>The parameter space is explored using MCMC techniques. The models are constrained by the spectra over six wavelength ranges: 4000\u20134900\u00a0\u00c5, 5050\u20135600\u00a0\u00c5, 5800\u20136700\u00a0\u00c5, 6700\u20138000\u00a0\u00c5, 8000\u20138900\u00a0\u00c5, and 9700\u201310200\u00a0\u00c5. (Small gaps between the first three regions match gaps in the IMACS spectra.) The latter range includes the Wing\u2013Ford FeH band and is not available for SNL-2 or our analysis of SDSS composite spectra. Within each wavelength range, a polynomial of order \u0394<em>\u03bb</em>/(100 \u00c5) is allowed to modulate the continuum shape of the models to best match the data.</p><h3 id=\"apjaa816ds6-1\" name=\"apjaa816ds6-1\">6.1.\u00a0Spectroscopic Constraints for SNELLS Lenses</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The resulting fits are illustrated in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa816df3\">3</a> for the case of the double power-law IMF. The quality of fit is very high: rms residuals are 0.6%, 0.8%, and 1.1% for the three lenses, respectively, compared to the rms formal errors of 0.4%, 0.5%, and 1.0% within the fitted regions (per 100 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> bin).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa816df3\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa816df3\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816df3_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 3.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 3.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816df3_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 3.</strong>\u00a0Spectra of the SNELLS lenses (colored lines) are compared to the best-fit SPS models (black lines). Residuals are shown in the lower panels, offset for clarity, with the bands encompassing the \u00b11<em>\u03c3</em> error spectrum. As described in the text, we plot models based on a double power-law IMF, but the fit quality is virtually identical for the other IMF parameterizations.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaa816df3\" href=\"/0004-637X/845/2/157/downloadFigure/figure/apjaa816df3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaa816df3\" href=\"/0004-637X/845/2/157/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaa816df3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>The spectroscopic constraints on <em>\u03b1</em> are summarized in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa816dt1\">1</a>. In Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa816df4\">4</a> we compare these constraints among the IMF parameterizations and to the lensing and dynamical estimates derived in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa816ds5\">5</a>. The spectroscopic estimates for the single and double power-law models are nearly indistinguishable for the SNELLS lenses. When a cutoff <em>m</em><sub>cut</sub> is included in the IMF parameterization or when a more general nonparametric form is used, <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em> declines by <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />20%, as we discuss further in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa816ds6-3\">6.3</a>. We note that La Barbera et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib36\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib36\">2013</a>) and Lyubenova et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib41\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib41\">2016</a>) found even larger dependences on the IMF form, but those studies considered a single slope extending from 0.6 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> to beyond 1 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>, which leads to variations in <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em> from both remnants and dwarfs.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa816df4\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa816df4\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816df4_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 4.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 4.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816df4_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 4.</strong>\u00a0Spectroscopic (colored points) and lensing/dynamical (black/gray points) constraints on the IMF are compared for the SNELLS lenses and an SDSS composite spectrum of ETGs with <em>\u03c3</em>\u00a0=\u00a0280 km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup>. The spectroscopic results are shown for the various parameterizations of the IMF indicated in the legend. (Note that the number of power-law segments refers to those below 1 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>; the slope at &gt;1 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> is fixed as described in the text.) For the SDSS stack, the gray square corresponds to <em>\u03b1</em> from the ATLAS<sup>3D</sup>+SLACS relation constructed by Posacki et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib56\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib56\">2015</a>), evaluated at <em>\u03c3</em><sub><em>e</em>/2</sub>\u00a0=\u00a0280 km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup> and converted from a Salpeter IMF into the reference Kroupa IMF used in this paper.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaa816df4\" href=\"/0004-637X/845/2/157/downloadFigure/figure/apjaa816df4\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaa816df4\" href=\"/0004-637X/845/2/157/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaa816df4\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>Given the additional complexities in the reduction of the FIRE spectra and our lack of a near-infrared spectrum of SNL-2, we have tested the sensitivity of our results to the inclusion of the spectral region around the Wing\u2013Ford band. Excluding this region has a negligible effect on the derived <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em> and <em>\u03b1</em> for SNL-0 and SNL-1. Furthermore, motivated by concerns about the accuracy of SPS models in matching the strengths of optical and near-infrared Na <span class=\"small-caps\">i</span> features (e.g., Smith et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib67\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib67\">2015a</a>), we have also performed fits excluding the line at 8190\u00a0\u00c5. As we show in Appendix\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa816dapp2\">B</a>, this lowers the inferred <em>\u03b1</em> by <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />30%, a significant effect that we return to when we discuss our interpretation in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa816ds7\">7</a>. The sensitivity of <em>\u03b1</em> to the Na <span class=\"small-caps\">i</span> 8190\u00a0\u00c5\u00a0feature and its relative insensitivity to the inclusion of the Wing\u2013Ford band are both consistent with the findings of <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib21\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib21\">CvD12</a> (see their Figure 12).</p><p>In general, a major hinderance in comparing IMF estimates is the matching of spatial apertures, which is very important given that the IMF may vary radially (Mart\u00edn-Navarro et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib43\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib43\">2015a</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib44\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib44\">2015b</a>; La Barbera et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib38\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib38\">2016</a>; McConnell et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib46\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib46\">2016</a>; Davis &amp; McDermid <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib25\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib25\">2017</a>; van Dokkum et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib80\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib80\">2017</a>; Zieleniewski et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib87\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib87\">2017</a>). A key strength of our SNELLS comparison is that aperture differences are very minimal, since the spectroscopic aperture is close to the Einstein radius.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa816dfn4\">9</a></sup>\n For SNL-1 and SNL-2, these radii are matched within \u22648%. For the worst case of SNL-0, where <em>\u03b8</em><sub>Ein</sub>\u00a0=\u00a02<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />85, we can roughly estimate the effect of an IMF gradient by assuming that it has a similar slope to that inferred by La Barbera et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib38\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib38\">2016</a>) for a single massive lens galaxy. For their measured gradient, our spectroscopic <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em> should be reduced by 8% to match the lensing aperture, which is not enough to substantially affect our comparisons. Therefore, the lensing, dynamical, and spectroscopic IMF estimates refer as closely as possible to the same aperture.</p><p>With all lensing, dynamical, and SPS estimates of <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em> for each galaxy, we can start to assess the mutual consistency of these techniques. For SNL-2, Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa816df4\">4</a> shows that all methods yield consistent results regardless of the modeling assumptions. For SNL-0 and SNL-1, the results of the consistency test depend on the assumed parameterization of the IMF and the dark matter contribution. We develop these comparisons in detail in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa816ds7\">7</a>. First, we develop a comparison sample that will prove useful for interpreting our SNELLS measurements.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaa816ds6-2\" name=\"apjaa816ds6-2\">6.2.\u00a0SNELLS Lenses Compared to a Composite ETG</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>All lensing estimates, regardless of the dark matter contribution, indicate that the SNELLS lenses have systematically low <em>\u03b1</em> for their velocity dispersions. It is therefore interesting to ask how the spectra and spectroscopically inferred <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em> of the SNELLS lenses compare with those of an average ETG with similar velocity dispersion.</p><p>For this purpose, we use a composite spectrum constructed from a carefully selected sample of ETGs drawn from the SDSS. ETGs are selected with cuts based on the stellar mass\u2013star formation rate relation and the equivalent widths of H<em>\u03b1</em> and [O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>]. The set of stacked spectra will be described and analyzed in a forthcoming paper (C. Conroy et al. 2017, in preparation). The stacks are created in bins of velocity dispersion that are 0.1\u00a0dex wide. For our purposes, we use a spectrum centered on <em>\u03c3</em>\u00a0=\u00a0280 km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup>, which is very close to the mean of the SNELLS sample, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816dieqn30.gif\" alt=\"$\\langle {\\sigma }_{e/2}\\rangle =288\\,\\mathrm{km}\\,{{\\rm{s}}}^{-1}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\langle {\\sigma }_{e/2}\\rangle =288\\,\\mathrm{km}\\,{{\\rm{s}}}^{-1}</script></span></span>.</p><p>Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa816df5\">5</a> presents a direct comparison between the mean spectrum of the SNELLS lenses and the SDSS stack. We have carefully interpolated the SNELLS spectra to the redshift and sampling of the SDSS stack, convolved them to match the SDSS line width (accounting for both velocity dispersion and instrumental resolution), and warped the SNELLS spectra to match the continuum shape of the SDSS stack before averaging them. Continuum shapes were matched by fitting the ratio spectrum using a cubic spline with knots spaced by 200\u00a0\u00c5. The median signal-to-noise ratio of the SDSS spectrum redward of Mg\u00a0b is 370 per 100\u00a0km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup> (equivalently, 250 per \u00c5).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa816df5\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa816df5\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816df5_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 5.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 5.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816df5_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 5.</strong>\u00a0Mean spectrum of the SNELLS lenses compared to our SDSS composite spectrum of ETGs with <em>\u03c3</em>\u00a0=\u00a0280 km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup>. The spectra have been matched in line width and continuum shape as described in the text. Residuals are shown in the lower panels, with the gray band indicating the \u00b11<em>\u03c3</em> formal uncertainty. The IMF-sensitive features identified by Spiniello et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib73\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib73\">2014</a>) are labeled. Overall, the spectra are remarkably similar; the rms differences are 0.32% per 100\u00a0km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup> bin.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaa816df5\" href=\"/0004-637X/845/2/157/downloadFigure/figure/apjaa816df5\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaa816df5\" href=\"/0004-637X/845/2/157/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaa816df5\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>Overall, the mean SNELLS and SDSS spectra are extremely similar and show an rms difference of only 0.32% (in 100 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> bins), comparable to the measurement errors. We note that differences around H<em>\u03b1</em> and H<em>\u03b2</em> arise from nebular emission in SNL-1, which is likely driven by the presence of warm gas rather than intrinsic differences in its stellar population. No visually significant differences in the IMF-sensitive regions marked in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa816df5\">5 </a>are evident at this signal-to-noise ratio. Correspondingly, when we fit the SDSS stack using the same models that were applied to the SNELLS lenses, we find that the inferred <em>\u03b1</em> is close to the mean of the SNELLS sample for each IMF parameterization (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa816df4\">4</a>, left panel).</p><p>As expected from the similarity of the spectra, the abundance patterns of the SNELLS lenses are typical. Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa816df6\">6</a> shows that in the mean, the SNELLS abundance ratios match those of the SDSS stack well. In particular, [Na/Fe], [Ca/Fe], and [Ti/Fe] agree very closely, which is relevant since most of the IMF-sensitive features involve these elements. Likewise, approximating the total metallicity as [<em>Z</em>/H]\u00a0=\u00a0[Fe/H]\u00a0+\u00a00.9 [Mg/Fe], we find that the SNELLS lenses have a mean <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816dieqn31.gif\" alt=\"$\\langle [{\\text{}}Z/{\\rm{H}}]\\rangle =0.26$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\langle [{\\text{}}Z/{\\rm{H}}]\\rangle =0.26</script></span></span> that is quite close to the value [<em>Z</em>/H]\u00a0=\u00a00.24 measured in the SDSS stack.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa816df6\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa816df6\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816df6_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 6.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 6.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816df6_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 6.</strong>\u00a0Abundance ratios of the SNELLS sample compared to the SDSS composite spectrum, demonstrating the typical abundance pattern of the SNELLS galaxies. Only elements with well-constrained abundances are plotted.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaa816df6\" href=\"/0004-637X/845/2/157/downloadFigure/figure/apjaa816df6\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaa816df6\" href=\"/0004-637X/845/2/157/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaa816df6\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div><h3 id=\"apjaa816ds6-3\" name=\"apjaa816ds6-3\">6.3.\u00a0The Influence of Priors and IMF Parameterizations on the Inference of <em>\u03b1</em></h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Our comparison of the masses and the stellar population properties of the SNELLS lenses and the SDSS stack has revealed an apparent contradiction. Lensing indicates a lighter IMF in the SNELLS lenses compared to the typical ETG of matched <em>\u03c3</em>, as determined by the ATLAS<sup>3D</sup>\u00a0and SLACS surveys. On the other hand, when the spectra are analyzed using the <em>same</em> IMF parameterization and priors, the inferred <em>\u03b1</em> is always consistent between the SNELLS sample (in the mean) and a composite SDSS spectrum of matched-<em>\u03c3</em> ETGs. Furthermore, independent of the stellar population modeling, the spectra themselves are very similar. Here we investigate a possible way to reconcile these observations.</p><p>Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa816df7\">7</a> shows the spectroscopically inferred IMFs for the SNELLS sample. As the complexity of the IMF parameterization is increased, either through the introduction of a low-mass cutoff or a fully nonparametric form, the models favor a smaller contribution of low-mass stars relative to the double power-law model. An important question is the extent to which this smaller contribution is driven by the priors versus the likelihoods (i.e., the data). For example, the prior on <em>m</em><sub>cut</sub> in the double power-law+cutoff parameterization is uniform between 0.08 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> and 0.4 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>. Thus, <em>m</em><sub>cut</sub> can only be higher than the canonical hydrogen-burning limit, and <em>\u03b1</em> can only decrease compared to the simpler model with a fixed low-mass cutoff. The decline in <em>\u03b1</em> could arise either because the data prefer a cutoff above the hydrogen-burning limit or because the data do not strongly constrain <em>m</em><sub>cut</sub>. In the latter case, the lower inferred <em>\u03b1</em> could be due entirely to marginalizing over 0.08 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>\u00a0&lt;\u00a0<em>m</em><sub>cut</sub>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.4 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>. Likewise, the priors on the IMF weights for the nonparametric model (see Conroy et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib22\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib22\">2017</a>) are centered on sub-Salpeter slopes, which could drive the inferred <em>\u03b1</em> downward (toward the prior) if the data do not strongly constrain the number of low-mass stars.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa816df7\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa816df7\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816df7_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 7.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 7.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816df7_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 7.</strong>\u00a0Inferred IMFs for the SNELLS lenses. Results are shown for three of the four IMF parameterizations, with the single power-law omitted for clarity. For the double power-law IMFs with (red lines) and without (blue lines) a variable low-mass cutoff, five posterior samples are shown to illustrate the uncertainties. For the nonparametric IMF (orange points), the IMF weight and its uncertainty is plotted in each of the mass bins described by Conroy et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib22\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib22\">2017</a>).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaa816df7\" href=\"/0004-637X/845/2/157/downloadFigure/figure/apjaa816df7\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaa816df7\" href=\"/0004-637X/845/2/157/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaa816df7\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>A robust model comparison can be made for one particular case of interest, the double power-law IMF parameterizations with and without a variable low-mass cutoff. This comparison is possible because the models are \"nested\": the latter is simply a special case of the former with <em>m</em><sub>cut</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.08 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>. In Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa816df8\">8</a> we show the marginalized posterior probability distributions for <em>m</em><sub>cut</sub> for the SNELLS lenses and the SDSS stack.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa816df8\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa816df8\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816df8_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 8.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 8.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816df8_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 8.</strong>\u00a0Marginalized posterior probability densities for <em>m</em><sub>cut</sub>, as inferred for the SNELLS lenses and the SDSS stack using the double power-law+cutoff IMF parameterization. The thick line is the probability distribution for the mean <em>m</em><sub>cut</sub> of the SNELLS ensemble, assuming that the lenses share a common value, and is obtained as the product of the posteriors of the individual lenses.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaa816df8\" href=\"/0004-637X/845/2/157/downloadFigure/figure/apjaa816df8\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaa816df8\" href=\"/0004-637X/845/2/157/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaa816df8\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>The posterior for the SDSS stack peaks near the hydrogen-burning limit; therefore, the decline in <em>\u03b1</em> when a variable low-mass cutoff is introduced (compare blue and red points in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa816df4\">4</a>) is not because the data demand <em>m</em><sub>cut</sub>\u00a0&gt;\u00a00.08 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>, but instead arises from marginalizing over elevated <em>m</em><sub>cut</sub> values that are disfavored but not ruled out. The situation is different for the SNELLS lenses. For each of the lenses, the posterior peaks above 0.08 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>, indicating a preference for an elevated cutoff mass, although this preference is not significant for individual systems. The thick line in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa816df8\">8</a> shows the product of the posterior probability densities, which is a constraint on the mean <em>m</em><sub>cut</sub> for the SNELLS ensemble. The posterior probability density at <em>m</em><sub>cut</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.15 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> is a factor of 10 higher than that at 0.08 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>. This is moderate evidence, but not decisive, in favor of an elevated cutoff mass from the spectra alone.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa816dfn5\">10</a></sup>\n\n</p><p>Is such a difference in the low-mass IMF consistent with the similarity of the mean SNELLS and SDSS spectra demonstrated in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa816ds6-2\">6.2</a>? In Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa816df9\">9</a> we focus on SNL-1 and compare the residuals from the best-fitting single power-law model (gray lines) to the difference between that model and the best-fitting nonparametric model (blue lines). These models were chosen because their corresponding IMF mass factors differ by \u0394<em>\u03b1</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.6, which is the largest dependence on parameterization that we find (see Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa816df4\">4</a>). At <em>\u03bb</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a05800 \u00c5, where most of the IMF constraining power lies, the two models never differ by more than 0.15% (excluding the H<em>\u03b1</em>+[N <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] region). This demonstrates that although the mean SNELLS and SDSS spectra differ very little, with an rms scatter of 0.32%, there are models based on different IMF parameterizations that differ even less (0.15%) and still have significantly different <em>\u03b1</em>.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa816df9\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa816df9\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816df9_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 9.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 9.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816df9_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 9.</strong>\u00a0Differences between the single power-law IMF and nonparametric IMF model fits to SNL-1 (blue line) are compared to the residuals from the single power-law model (gray) in the lower panels. The shaded region denotes the \u00b11<em>\u03c3</em> measurement uncertainties. The upper panel in each figure repeats the SNL-1 spectrum from Figure <a href=\"#apjaa816df3\">3</a>. The differences between the best-fit models are very small\u2014much less than the residuals\u2014despite a difference in the IMF mass factor of \u0394<em>\u03b1</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.6 between them.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaa816df9\" href=\"/0004-637X/845/2/157/downloadFigure/figure/apjaa816df9\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaa816df9\" href=\"/0004-637X/845/2/157/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaa816df9\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>Taken at face value, the small differences between these models can still be detected when aggregated over the full spectrum of a galaxy: comparing these two models, denoted <em>m</em><sub>1</sub> and <em>m</em><sub>2</sub>, we find <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/845/2/157/Full/apjaa816dieqn32.gif\" alt=\"${\\rm{\\Delta }}{\\chi }^{2}={{\\rm{\\Sigma }}}_{i}{({m}_{1,i}-{m}_{2,i})}^{2}/{\\sigma }_{i}^{2}\\,=\\,8$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\rm{\\Delta }}{\\chi }^{2}={{\\rm{\\Sigma }}}_{i}{({m}_{1,i}-{m}_{2,i})}^{2}/{\\sigma }_{i}^{2}\\,=\\,8</script></span></span>, where the sum is over spectral pixels. However, such small differences can only be detected statistically and are not easily ascribed to individual spectral features (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa816df9\">9</a>). Moreover, there are likely systematic errors in the models at the ~0.5% level (e.g., van Dokkum et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib80\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib80\">2017</a>, Figure 8), which makes the interpretation of such small spectral differences uncertain. This makes it difficult to establish a definitive difference in the low-mass IMF of the SNELLS lenses and the SDSS stack from the spectra alone.</p><p>In summary, there is moderate evidence for a difference in the low-mass (<em>m</em>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a00.3 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>) IMFs of the SNELLS lenses compared to the SDSS stack. This is not inconsistent with the similarity of their spectra because the model differences can be even smaller. Although the spectra alone cannot decisively establish such a difference, the truncated IMF they suggest in the SNELLS lenses could explain their lower <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em> (relative to the mean matched-<em>\u03c3</em> ETG) inferred using lensing.</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa816ds7\" name=\"apjaa816ds7\">7.\u00a0Discussion</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><h3 id=\"apjaa816ds7-1\" name=\"apjaa816ds7-1\">7.1.\u00a0Summary and Comparison of IMF Constraints</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We have presented new observations of the three low-redshift (<em>z</em> = 0.03\u20130.05) ETG strong lenses located in the SNELLS survey (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib69\">S15</a>). The presence of strong lensing and the feasibility of very high-quality spectroscopy make them excellent systems for testing the consistency of IMF estimates derived from lensing, stellar dynamics, and stellar population synthesis.</p><p>The most fundamental and robust test is in the limit of no dark matter: does the SPS-based <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em> exceed the total <em>M</em>/<em>L</em>? SNL-2 clearly satisfies this test. For SNL-0 and SNL-1, the results of the test depend on the IMF parameterization. When using a single or double power-law model that extends down to 0.08 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>,<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa816dfn6\">11</a></sup>\n the spectroscopic <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em> exceeds the <em>total</em> lensing mass. The significance of this difference is 2.5<em>\u03c3</em> and 1.9<em>\u03c3</em> for SNL-0 and SNL-1, respectively, for the double power-law model. Models with more flexibility at the low-mass end of the IMF largely eliminate this tension. For the double power-law model with a variable low-mass cutoff, the differences with respect to the the total lensing masses are reduced to 1.7<em>\u03c3</em> and 1.0<em>\u03c3</em> for SNL-0 and SNL-1, respectively, while for our nonparametric model, these differences are only 1.1<em>\u03c3</em> and 0.1<em>\u03c3</em>. The lensing masses are more precise and less sensitive to the modeling assumptions (e.g., velocity anisotropy) than the dynamical masses. Nonetheless, if we consider the total dynamical <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> for SNL-0, we find that it is also more consistent with the SPS estimates based on the more flexible IMF parameterizations, although the tension with the single and double power-law models is lessened. Altogether, the SPS-based <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em> does not exceed the <em>total</em> lensing or dynamical masses for the SNELLS lenses, if they have a deficit of low-mass stars relative to the double power-law model. This scenario is moderately favored by the spectra (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa816df8\">8</a>).</p><p>Turning to comparisons of the stellar <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em>, the consistency between lensing/dynamical and SPS estimates depends on the dark matter fraction, which we have evaluated in two ways. First, considering the lensing masses after subtracting a dark matter contribution estimated using the EAGLE simulations, we find that (<em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em>)<sub>L+EAGLE</sub> and SPS estimates differ by 4.6<em>\u03c3</em> and 3.1<em>\u03c3</em> for SNL-0 and SNL-1, respectively, assuming a single power-law IMF; 3.4<em>\u03c3</em> and 2.7<em>\u03c3</em> for a double power-law IMF; 2.7<em>\u03c3</em> and 1.9<em>\u03c3</em> for a double power-law IMF with a low-mass cutoff; or 1.9<em>\u03c3</em> and 1.5<em>\u03c3</em> for the nonparametric IMF model. For SNL-2, all methods of IMF estimation are consistent regardless of the dark matter contribution or IMF parameterization. Notably, all discrepancies are reduced to &lt;2<em>\u03c3</em> when considering models based on the nonparametric IMF.</p><p>Second, we have independently estimated the dark matter fraction via a joint lensing+dynamical analysis for SNL-0 and SNL-2. The resulting <em>\u03b1</em> closely matches the lensing+EAGLE-based estimate for SNL-0, and correspondingly, the dark matter fraction <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub> is consistent with the EAGLE estimate and inconsistent with a scenario with no dark matter at the 2.3<em>\u03c3</em> level. The main systematic uncertainty arises from the black hole mass and orbital structure, as discussed in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa816ds4-3\">4.3</a>, but variations to our default model mainly shift <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em> downward and thus would increase tension with the SPS estimates. For SNL-2, although the <em>\u03b1</em> inferred from lensing+dynamics is higher than the EAGLE-based estimate, both are consistent with the SPS estimates for all IMF parameterizations.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaa816ds7-2\" name=\"apjaa816ds7-2\">7.2.\u00a0Variations at the Low-mass End of the IMF?</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>A key result of this paper is that consistency between lensing, dynamical, and spectroscopic <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em> estimates requires that in two of the SNELLS lenses, the IMF below 0.5 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> is not a power law extending down to the canonical hydrogen-burning limit of 0.08 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>. Additional flexibility at the low-mass end (<em>m</em>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a00.3 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>) is needed, at least in the context of the CvD models and full-spectrum fitting techniques used here. When this flexibility is provided in the form of our nonparametric IMF, the spectroscopic <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em> is within 2<em>\u03c3</em> of (<em>M</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>L</em>)<sub>L+EAGLE</sub>. This mild discrepancy may be reduced if the SNELLS lenses have a smaller dark matter contribution than suggested by simulations, as the lensing+dynamics analysis suggests for SNL-2. Furthermore, since we know that the SNELLS galaxies have an atypical total <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> for their <em>\u03c3</em>, it is reasonable to suppose that their formation histories and dark matter fractions could also be atypical.</p><p>In addition to better agreement with lensing and dynamical measures, analyses of the spectra alone favor a reduced contribution of low-mass stars, either via an elevated low-mass cutoff <em>m</em><sub>cut</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.15\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.03 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> in models parameterized as such, or via a turnover in the IMF at <em>m</em>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a00.3 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> in our nonparametric IMF models. The evidence from the spectra alone is only of moderate significance and not definitive, but it becomes more interesting when coupled with consistent evidence for a lightweight IMF provided by lensing/dynamics. If we accept these results at face value and assume that systematic errors in the methods are not dominant, then this physical scenario appears to be the most plausible one that can reconcile the IMF inferences for all methods.</p><p>If the SNELLS lenses have a reduced contribution of low-mass stars, this may point to diversity in the low-mass IMF rather than a general property of high-<em>\u03c3</em> ETGs (see also Leier et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa816dbib40\" id=\"fnref-apjaa816dbib40\">2016</a>). All of our lensing-based estimates, regardless of dark matter fraction, imply that all of the SNELLS lenses have a lighter IMF than the mean matched-<em>\u03c3</em> galaxy in the ATLAS<sup>3D</sup> and SLACS surveys (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa816df4\">4</a>). Only if we adopt the total dynamical mass <em>and</em> assume there is no dark matter would the SNELLS lenses' IMF be consistent with the IMF of the mean matched-<em>\u03c3</em> galaxy. Considering the limitations of our stellar kinematic constraints and analysis, we judge that the lensing constraints are more robust. They are certainly more precise, and the dynamical measures are consistent with them at the 1<em>\u03c3</em> level. Improved stellar kinematic data (such as wide-field integral field spectroscopy) and a more sophisticated dynamical analysis (such as Schwarzschild modeling) could provide a yet more stringent test of the consistency of the masses.</p><p>While lensing/dynamics measures imply that the SNELLS lenses have atypically light IMFs and therefore that there is scatter in the IMF of high-<em>\u03c3</em> ETGs, these techniques cannot elucidate the nature of the such variations.
